# DA Link Toolbox

## China Soft Power DA

### Link\-\--1NC

#### US arctic presence will threaten China

Nong **Hong 25**, Dr. Nong Hong is Executive Director at the Institute
for China-America Studies, and Senior Fellow at Beijing Club for
International Dialogue. Previously, she was also Senior Fellow at
National Institute for the South China Sea Studies. She holds a PhD of
interdisciplinary study of international law and international relations
from the University of Alberta, Canada, ICAS, 6/2/25, "US, China and EU:
The race for Greenland's mineral riches,"
https://chinaus-icas.org/research/us-china-and-eu-the-race-for-greenlands-mineral-riches/?utm_source,
igo

China's cautious yet persistent presence

[[China has]{.mark} long [eyed Greenland as]{.mark}]{.underline} part of
its [[Arctic economic strategy]{.mark}.]{.underline} Its 2018 Arctic
Policy White Paper declared China as "Arctic stakeholder" and
highlighted scientific research, infrastructure, energy and mineral
development as key interests. [Chinese firms, such as Shenghe
Resources]{.underline} and China National Nuclear Corporation (CNNC),
[have attempted to invest in Greenlandic mining]{.underline}, notably in
the Kvanefjeld and Isua Iron Ore Project Mine.

However, these [efforts have encountered political
headwinds]{.underline}. [Greenland's]{.underline} 2021 [uranium ban was
widely interpreted as a rebuke to Chinese involvement.]{.underline} In
2019, Danish intelligence reportedly warned that China sought to
establish dual-use infrastructure in Greenland that could serve
strategic or military purposes. This has increased Western wariness
about Chinese investment.

Nonetheless, [[China remains engaged in Greenland]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Its [[firms continue to hold minority stakes in]{.mark}]{.underline}
certain [[projects]{.underline}]{.mark} and explore partnerships.
Beijing's global Belt and Road Initiative and growing Arctic diplomacy
make Greenland a continued focal point for resource diplomacy. As global
demand for REEs intensifies, China may recalibrate its strategy ---
balancing commercial interest with lower-profile engagement to avoid
geopolitical friction.

Despite these constraints, [[Greenland has signalled]{.mark}
a]{.underline} pragmatic [[openness to Chinese
investment]{.mark},]{.underline} warning that "if the US and EU do not
act, [we may turn to Chinese investment]{.underline}". This reflects
Nuuk's realist approach to foreign engagement --- China remains a viable
partner, provided it adheres to Greenland's conditions, including
respect for sovereignty, environmental safeguards, and transparency.

Greenland welcomed the EU's initiative, but Nathanielsen noted that the
bloc has yet to translate its intentions into tangible investments.

The EU's green demand and regulatory leverage

The EU pursues a different path --- leveraging rules and norms to
project soft power. Its 2020 Critical Raw Materials Action Plan and 2023
Critical Raw Materials Act aim to diversify and secure sustainable
mineral supplies. Greenland, with its close historical ties to Denmark,
is seen as a key non-Chinese source of rare earths in the EU's strategic
calculus.

Unlike the US security-driven model, the EU emphasises environmental
protection, corporate social responsibility and Indigenous consultation.
The 2023 EU-Greenland MOU on Sustainable Raw Materials Value Chains
outlines cooperation on feasibility studies, environmental assessments,
and local stakeholder engagement.

Greenland welcomed the EU's initiative, but Nathanielsen noted that the
bloc has yet to translate its intentions into tangible investments. What
Greenland needs, she stressed, are real partners --- not just verbal
commitments.

Geopolitical and environmental implications

Greenland's mining politics encapsulate broader Arctic shifts. [[As
great powers increase]{.mark} their economic
[footprints]{.mark},]{.underline} the [[island becomes a proxy arena for
strategic competition]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[US-China rivalry is
increasingly mirrored]{.underline}]{.mark} in resource diplomacy, while
the EU acts as a normative power pushing environmental safeguards.

This dynamic raises two concerns. First, [[Greenland may become
over-politicised]{.underline}]{.mark}. As its autonomy within the
Kingdom of Denmark grows, Nuuk seeks to chart its own economic path. Yet
external pressure risks reducing Greenlandic agency, especially if
caught in US-China tension.

[Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline} (Sorensen, 2018, p. 84). [[Greenland's

and]{.mark} to [have a foothold]{.mark} for Arctic access.]{.underline}
The Chinese state is building an Arctic knowledge which can be analysed
to facilitate investment in Greenland in the future, as well as support
China's full participation in the Arctic matters. (Andersson et al.,

[[Greenland]{.mark} is expected to [play a **crucial** role in China's
emerging Arctic strategy]{.mark} because of its proximity to the Arctic
shipping passages and potential for mineral resource
exploration]{.underline}, but this evaluation is based on academic
guesses. The main condition for more significant Chinese economic

favourable. Also, greater agreements between China and Greenland

88). In general, China has operated in the Arctic carefully, and it is
still an open question of how strong of a partner would China become
with Greenland (Sorensen, 2018, p. 90). [There is an additional United
States component, since it is Denmark's strategically closest ally and
has a long-standing vision for the military-strategic presence in

Therefore, [a greater presence of China in Greenland is monitored
closely by the United States.]{.underline} (Sorensen, 2018, p. 84;

(Zeuthen & Raftopoulos, 2018, p. 122). Also, [[the development of
the]{.mark} [Arctic governance **interests** China]{.mark}]{.underline}
since it is a question of distinguishing international and national
waters, which has an impact on the position China has in the Arctic.
(Sorensen, 2018, p. 89)

### AT: China Not Involved\-\--2NC

#### China and Greenland have [close]{.underline} economic ties

Flemming **Christiansen 25**, a mining and geology consultant with a
focus on new research and exploration projects in Greenland, IEEE
Spectrum, 5/8/25, "Can Geopolitics Unlock Greenland's Critical Materials
Treasure Chest?"
https://spectrum.ieee.org/greenland-rare-earth-minerals, igo

After the Trump administration touched off a tariff war, the
geopolitical skirmishing between the United States and China over rare
earths became a much more complicated conflagration involving the United
States, the European Union, and China.

And the Greenland government has continued to fan the flames by courting
Chinese investment. In March, for example, [[Greenland's foreign
minister]{.mark}, Vivian Motzfeldt]{.underline}, reportedly [[identified
closer cooperation with China]{.underline}]{.mark} as a priority,
[and]{.underline} even [touted the possibility of a free trade agreement
between Greenland and China.]{.underline} According to a report in The
Diplomat, Motzfeldt's actions were "largely driven by the belief...that
a mining boom, fueled by Chinese investment, was the most realistic path
toward independence from Denmark---a goal shared by most Greenlandic
parties." Maybe so, but such moves have been widely perceived as a
provocation against Denmark and the United States. Whatever their
motivation, they **[[could]{.mark} very well [lead to increased U.S.
pressure]{.mark}]{.underline}**. [For the U]{.underline}nited
[S]{.underline}tates [it would be a [geostrategic nightmare if China
opened a mine]{.mark} in a remote part of Greenland]{.underline}, with a
town, communication lines, harbor, and an airfield that could obviously
be used for purposes other than resource extraction and export.

Since Greenland took control of its mineral resources, Denmark has been
reluctant to get involved in mining projects. That could very well
change. Denmark and the E.U. may come under pressure to invest in
much-needed infrastructure and energy projects and offer loans on more
favorable conditions, if for no other reason than to keep the United
States at bay.

Though the situation is highly unstable, it's important to try, at
least, to separate the geopolitical posturing from the realities of
mining. In that vein, it's safe to say there will be no operating rare
earth mines in Greenland during the term of Donald J. Trump.

#### China is NOT leaving Greenland

Marc **Jacobsen** et al, 20**24**, Associate Professor at Royal Danish
Defense College, 2/13/24, "Greenland in Arctic security:
(De)securitization Dynamics under Climatic Thaw and Geopolitical
Freeze," https://doi.org/10.3998/mpub.12676130, igo

[[Chinese interest in Greenland **has grown**]{.mark} in the past
decade.]{.underline} [[Examples include investments]{.underline} [in
potential]{.underline}]{.mark} [[mining projects]{.underline}]{.mark}
(Andersson, Zeuthen, and Kalvig 2018), a bid by a large state-owned
company to assist in the refurbishment of airports (Simpson 2018;
Sejersen, chap. 9, this vol.), [and a]{.underline} controversial
[attempt by a Chinese mining company to acquire an]{.underline}
abandoned [Danish naval base]{.underline} (Breum 2016) to facilitate its
activities in Greenland. [[Greenland's rich mineral deposits
have]{.underline}]{.mark} been a focus [of [Chinese
interest]{.mark}.]{.underline} In 2016, a Chinese company partly owned
by a subdivision of China Geological Survey invested in one of
Greenland's potential rare earth projects, the Kuannersuit (Kvanefjeld)
project. Rare earth elements, often referred to as the 'vitamins of
modern industry,' are considered essential in the EU, the United States,
China, Japan, and many other countries because of their importance in
producing a wide range of high-tech products, including advanced
communications and consumer technologies, emerging 'green' technologies,
and advanced military weapons. Then, in 2017, [[Chinese activities in
the Arctic]{.mark} [received a]{.mark}n]{.underline} additional
[[boost]{.underline} [when the region was]{.underline}]{.mark}
[officially **[incorporated into the]{.mark} Belt and Road Initiative
([BRI)]{.mark}**---[China's]{.mark}]{.underline} signature [[foreign
policy]{.underline}]{.mark} and strategy for international
[[development]{.underline}]{.mark}---with the introduction of a 'Polar
Silk Road.' As discussed by Jacobsen and Olsvig (chap. 4, this vol.),
the U.S. has responded with a series of exceptional measures aimed at
countering Chinese influence in Greenland, measures that point to a
resecuritization of the country in U.S. politics. Although attempts at
securitizing U.S. interests in Greenland are shaped in part by
perceptions of China's Arctic strategy and security agenda in the
region, [very little is known about the degree to which Greenland or the
Arctic]{.underline} more broadly [are]{.underline} in fact [viewed as
security priorities in Chinese politics]{.underline}. Vuori (2008) has
argued that in the Chinese context, securitization, that is, the
justification of extraordinary political intervention to mitigate a
potential threat, is reserved for issues that are perceived as
existential threats to the survival of the Chinese Communist Party
(CCP). It would not appear that anything close to this would emerge from
conflicts in or about Greenland in any near future. This, however, does
not mean that Chinese engagement in Greenland is not enabled by forms of
policymaking that are framed as exceptional in China's authoritarian
system.

#### China is [active]{.underline} in Greenland's mines

Andrew **Langer 25**, President of the Institute for Liberty, Director
of the CPAC Foundation's Center for Regulatory Freedom, former Head of
Regulatory Practice at the National Federation of Independent Business
(NFIB), Bachelor's in International Relations from the College of
William & Mary, Master's in Public Administration from Troy University,
" 1/22/25, "Greenland: A Geostrategic and Economic Imperative for the
United States,"
https://www.cpac.org/post/greenland-a-geostrategic-and-economic-imperative-for-the-united-states,
igo

While the [[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates has been]{.underline}
relatively [[slow to recognize Greenland]{.mark}'s
importance]{.underline}, [[Russia and China have been more
proactive.]{.underline}]{.mark} Russia's Arctic ambitions are evident in
its extensive military build-up, which includes the establishment of new
airbases, missile installations, and icebreaker fleets. These
developments pose a direct threat to Greenland and, by extension, to
U.S. and NATO interests in the region.

China's approach, though less overtly militaristic, is no less
concerning. [[Through]{.mark} its Belt and Road Initiative
([BRI]{.mark}), [Beijing has sought to integrate Arctic]{.mark}
infrastructure [into its]{.mark}]{.underline} global [[economic
network]{.underline}]{.mark}. [[Chinese companies
have]{.underline}]{.mark} repeatedly [[attempted to **invest in
Greenland's mining**]{.underline}]{.mark}, energy, and transportation
sectors, often with financial terms that could lead to economic
dependency. If successful, these efforts would give China a strategic
foothold in the Arctic, undermining U.S. influence and creating
vulnerabilities in Western supply chains.

The [United States must]{.underline} adopt a multi-pronged strategy to
counter these threats. First, it should enhance its military presence in
Greenland and the broader Arctic region, ensuring that NATO's northern
flank remains secure. Second, it must work with Greenland and Denmark to
block Chinese investments that pose a risk to national security.
Finally, the U.S. should [invest in]{.underline} Arctic research and
development, infrastructure, and [**resource exploration** to strengthen
its position as a leader]{.underline} in the region.

[S]{.mark}tates [will continue to]{.mark} consistently [advance]{.mark}
[its geo-strategic, military-political, and economic interests in the

The U.S. first took an interest in acquiring Greenland in 1867. Shortly
after completing the purchase of Alaska from the Russian Empire,
then--Secretary of State William Seward set his sights on acquiring
Greenland from Denmark. The two countries never reached an agreement.
Later, after World War II, President Harry Truman offered Denmark \$100
million in gold and a portion of Alaskan oil. That offer was rebuffed.

[During World War II, the U.S. had several military bases in
Greenland]{.underline} that surveilled the Axis powers and were meant to
be a first line of defense against any potential westward expansion on
their behalf. [The U.S. continues to maintain bases in
Greenland]{.underline}.

[Putin alluded]{.underline} to the possibility [that [if the U.S. were
to take control of Greenland, it would **escalate** tensions with
Russia]{.mark}. He accused NATO countries of using the Arctic as a
"springboard for possible conflicts."]{.underline}

## Politics DA

### Link\-\--1NC

#### Minerals legislation get caught up in partisan fights.

John **Haughey 24**, reporter for the Epoch Times, 12/24/2024, "Congress
Failed to Pass Any Critical Minerals Bill During 2023-24 Sessions,"
https://www.theepochtimes.com/us/congress-failed-to-pass-any-critical-minerals-bill-during-2023-24-sessions-5781131

Stymied by Definition

Facing these trade war actions, [Congress did **little**]{.underline} in
its waning days before adjourning [to address economic and national
defense priorities it had deliberated the last two years]{.underline}.

[It left a parcel of bills related to]{.underline} overall [**energy
development** on the table]{.underline}, including measures that can
streamlined the permitting process despite a pan-industry, bipartisan
rally cry.

Most notably idling for more than a year was HR 1, The Lower Energy
Costs Act, essentially a blueprint for a House GOP "drill baby drill"
energy policy that the Democrat-led Senate never heard in committee.

The proposed Energy Permitting Reform Act of 2024---co-sponsored by
Sens. Joe Manchin (I-W.Va.) and John Barrasso (R-Wyo.) and lauded by
energy and mining associations as a good first machete hack into the
regulatory thicket---died unheard in the House.

[A **significant component** of hearings was dedicated to getting the
Department of Energy (**DOE**) and **USGS** on the **same page** in
defining **what is a critical mineral** and how the federal government
should]{.underline} comprehensively [assess sources, supply chains, and
end-uses in developing a strategy]{.underline}.

Among bills seeking to define critical minerals, HB 8446, [The Critical
Mineral Consistency Act]{.underline} of 2024, advanced the furthest.

Sponsored by six Republicans led by Rep. Juan Ciscomani (R-Ariz.), it
amends The Energy Act of 2020 to require the Interior secretary to
submit an annual report comprehensively assessing all global critical
mineral and rare earth element resources and to harmonize minerals
definitions and listings used by the DOE and the USGS.

It [spiraled into]{.underline} boxed canyon [**partisanship** by
including **copper** as a critical mineral, which Democrats objected to
as a "gift to the copper industry]{.underline} and the Chinese Communist
Party (CCP)," noting Resolution Copper, owned by Rio Tinto, a global
mining corporation, is proposing a copper mine in New Mexico.

"And guess who owns the largest stake in Rio Tinto?" Rep. Jared Huffman
(D-Calif.) asked. "The Chinese government."

HB 8446 was among the bills the House Rules Committee moved to the floor
on Nov. 12. The proposal passed the House on Nov. 14 in a 245-155 vote
and was never heard in the Senate where the copper inclusion nixed its
passage.

#### Trump's interest may boost short-term political momentum, but Greenland's harsh terrain, labor limits, and investor fears ensure mining hype won't translate into lasting success.

**Kantchev '25** \[Georgi and Julie Steinberg; January 2; Foreign
correspondent for The Wall Street Journal; Tovima, "Why Getting
Greenland's Mineral Riches Won't Be Easy,"
tovima.com/wsj/why-getting-greenlands-mineral-riches-wont-be-easy\]
//jwh

Still, despite all the interest in
**[[Greenland]{.mark}'s]{.underline}** potential, the island [[has **two
active** mines]{.mark}, and they aren't [**involved** in]{.mark} the
**extraction** of [critical **minerals**]{.mark}]{.underline}. The
territory has extended around 100 active mining licenses, mostly for
exploration, with some further along.

High startup costs are a particular issue: [[**Any** mine]{.mark} would
[have to be **built** from **scratch**]{.mark}]{.underline} in
Greenland's rugged terrain, [[with]{.mark} its **deep fjords** and
**[ice sheets]{.mark}** that can be [up to a **mile
thick**]{.mark}]{.underline}. The island doesn't have roads between
settlements, while the floating sea ice off the coast makes shipping
treacherous. The island's population of around 57,000 people likely
means importing foreign workers.

Greenland's decision to ban mining for uranium in recent years also
spooked some investors, who want to see successful projects come to
fruition before plowing in financing.

"[[There has always been a **lot of hype**]{.mark}, a **lot of
talk**]{.underline} of the potential there," said Flemming Getreuer
Christiansen , a geoscience consultant with decades of experience in the
region. "[[Trump's **interest**]{.mark} [**may** wake up interest
**now**, but the **challenges** remain]{.mark} the
**same**]{.underline}."

## Diplomatic Capital DA

### Link\-\--1NC

#### Greenland mining requires [intense]{.underline} diplomatic resources 

Gracelin **Baskaran 25**, a mining economist, is the director of the
Critical Minerals Security Program at the Center for Strategic and
International Studies, 3/4/25, The New York Times, "How Trump Can Turn
the Ukraine Mineral Deal Into Real Security,"
https://www.nytimes.com/2025/03/04/opinion/ukraine-minerals-deal.html?scrlybrkr=64bcc39a,
igo

Whether the Ukraine deal is eventually signed or not, [[incorporating
minerals into]{.mark} foreign [policy is]{.mark}]{.underline} crucial
for U.S. national security. However, without [[**dedicating**
government]{.mark} investment and **[diplomatic
resources]{.mark}**]{.underline} --- [as China has done]{.underline} ---
this initiative remains a hollow effort and may fail to deliver any
results.

With less than 2 percent of the world's reserves of rare earths,
graphite, cobalt and nickel, [[the U]{.underline}]{.mark}nited
[[S]{.underline}]{.mark}tates [[must **work closely**]{.mark} [with
resource-rich nations]{.mark}]{.underline} to make sure American
companies can get the minerals they need to build, among other things,
phones, batteries for electric vehicles and semiconductors. [[China
has]{.underline}]{.mark} similar challenges and has [[made minerals
diplomacy central to its foreign policy.]{.underline}]{.mark} Despite
accounting for only 1 percent to 10 percent of global lithium, cobalt,
nickel and copper production, China imports enough to process more than
65 percent of some of these metals and 90 percent of rare earths. This
level of control is the outcome of years of strategic industrial
planning and foreign policy efforts by Beijing.

Mr. Trump appears to be taking a page from China's playbook of active
minerals diplomacy. The draft agreement with Ukraine would reportedly
create a fund controlled by the United States and Ukraine to receive
future revenue from Ukraine's natural resources. But if it is signed, it
is not clear whether such an agreement will actually enhance U.S.
mineral security. In fact, it will be decades before we see the impact
of this agreement, if at all.

The United States has seemingly minimal knowledge of Ukraine's
underground resources. There is no modern mapping of the country's rare
earth deposits; the most recent surveys are believed to have been
conducted 30 to 60 years ago by what was then the Soviet Union. Without
up-to-date geological data, it is impossible to determine whether these
resources are economically viable for extraction. If the ore grade is
too low, the deposits are too small or the byproducts aren't valuable
enough, private companies are unlikely to invest the \$500 million to
\$1 billion needed to develop a mine and separation plant.

Moreover, under the draft agreement, Ukraine would be required to pay a
percentage of proceeds from newly developed mineral assets into a
reconstruction investment fund with joint U.S. and Ukraine ownership;
existing mineral, oil and gas operations are to remain exempt. Given
that the average time to develop a mine from resource discovery to
production is approximately 18 years, it will take at least that long
--- four more U.S. presidential election cycles --- before the United
States can begin sourcing minerals from Ukraine.

[There are ways to make [minerals diplomacy]{.mark}]{.underline} more
[effective]{.underline}, [but it [will require]{.mark}]{.underline} a
willingness by [[the U.S.]{.mark} [government]{.mark}]{.underline} not
just [[to]{.mark} strike deals]{.underline} but [also to [**spend and
invest**, over a long period]{.mark},]{.underline} in countries that
have the mineral resources we need. That may be a tough sell at a time
of fiscal austerity and budget cuts to the federal government. But China
did not build the significant competitive advantage it holds in electric
vehicle manufacturing by reducing spending. One analysis found that
between 2009 and 2023, the Chinese government allocated at least \$230.9
billion in subsidies to help develop the nascent industry. The fruits of
that effort are apparent in the domination of BYD's electric vehicles
worldwide.

So how should the American government spend the resources needed to
build a competitive minerals diplomacy strategy? First, the United
States should increase the capacity of the U.S. Geological Survey to
conduct geological mapping and reduce exploration risks in key regions.
Many resource-rich countries remain unmapped or reliant on outdated
surveys. The U.S. Geological Survey could also place their attachés in
embassies, to work alongside geological surveys and mining ministries in
host countries, promoting resource development strategies that are
favorable to Western investors.

Second, the United States needs to help build roads, bridges and other
infrastructure in mineral-rich places. Mining is one of the most
energy-intensive sectors in the world. Globally, it accounts for 38
percent of industrial energy use. One of the challenges with mining in
Ukraine, for example, is the fact that missile and drone strikes have
damaged about half of that country's power substations. To ease the way
for Western mining companies in Ukraine, the United States should
increase funding to institutions like the U.S. International Development
Finance Corporation, which can help rebuild some of those assets.

Any government-to-government cooperation agreement is useful only if it
stimulates investment by private mining companies, which develop those
minerals into a form that companies like Apple and Tesla can use. But
the world's most resource-rich nations, such as the Democratic Republic
of Congo, are not easy places for businesses to operate. The U.S.
government can help mitigate those risks by supporting mining project
negotiations and dispute resolution, and providing financing at
below-market rates and political risk insurance.

China has long aligned its infrastructure investments to mineral
investments. The 2007 Sino-Congolais des Mines agreement is a key
example of a resource-for-infrastructure deal. Under this arrangement,
Chinese companies gained access to cobalt, copper and other minerals in
exchange for the development of infrastructure, such as roads and
hospitals. The Chinese consortium pledged more than \$6.5 billion for
infrastructure projects, securing mining rights to deposits near Kolwezi
in the southeastern region of the Democratic Republic of Congo, which
were estimated to be worth \$93 billion. As a result, China now owns or
has stakes in 15 of Congo's biggest copper and cobalt mines.

The United States needs to come up with a blueprint for how minerals
diplomacy can play out in other parts of the world, not just Ukraine.
The Democratic Republic of Congo recently proposed that it could offer
the United States and Europe access to its mineral reserves in return
for military assistance, amid its ongoing conflict with neighboring
Rwanda. Such an agreement is well worth considering and, if it is
pursued, should be backed up with real investment.

Mining is a long and costly process. If [[America]{.mark}]{.underline}
is to be a global leader in the minerals it needs for national, economic
and energy security, it [[will have]{.mark} [to go beyond signing
agreements]{.mark}]{.underline}. In Ukraine and elsewhere, [[strategic
diplomacy]{.underline}]{.mark} and substantive investment [[will]{.mark}
[determine whether]{.mark}]{.underline} such
[[deals]{.underline}]{.mark} actually [[deliver]{.mark}]{.underline} the
[[mineral security]{.underline}]{.mark} the United States is looking
for.

#### Greenland mining requires diplomatic capital, China proves

Benjamin **Schmitt 20**, a postdoctoral fellow at Harvard University, a
senior fellow at the Center for European Policy Analysis, and a former
European Energy Security advisor at the US Department of State, a term
member of the Council on Foreign Relations, co-founder of the Duke Space
Diplomacy Lab, a senior fellow for Democratic Resilience at the Center
for European Policy Analysis (CEPA), 11/19/20, Atlantic Council, "*How
critical science infrastructure investments help put Arctic security
challenges on ice,"*
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/how-critical-science-infrastructure-investments-help-put-arctic-security-challenges-on-ice/,
igo

Moreover, [Beijing has pursued]{.underline} economic
[investment]{.underline} [in]{.underline} the critical infrastructure
and extractive industries across [the transatlantic Arctic]{.underline};
often employing aggressive tactics including predatory lending and
sovereign loan guarantee structures long common in China's engagement
with African and South American nations and, more recently, along NATO's
Eastern Flank. The [[Chinese]{.mark} [government]{.mark} [has expended
**significant diplomatic capital** to]{.mark} develop strategic
[investments in Greenland]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[engaging with]{.mark}
the [Greenlandic government]{.mark}]{.underline} [and local
communities]{.underline}, in part [[to **solidify**
its]{.underline}]{.mark} strong [position in the]{.underline} rare-earth
and critical [[mineral extractives industry]{.underline}]{.mark}
worldwide. These investment attempts are screened by Danish officials
involved in Greenland's foreign policy and national security process,
who together have recognized that significant involvement of Chinese
state-owned enterprises in the Greenlandic economy would enable Beijing
to wield influence over Greenland's affairs and have de facto influence
on Arctic Council governance decisions. All while Chinese firm
investments totaled nearly 12 percent of the Greenlandic gross domestic
product between 2012-2017.

Like its existing and planned economic investments in Arctic critical
infrastructure developed through the "Polar Silk Road," [Beijing has led
the development]{.underline} and deployment [of]{.underline}
significant, permanent scientific research stations and [infrastructure
across the northern Atlantic]{.underline}, [thereby increasing its
physical presence across the region]{.underline}---including on the
territory of NATO Allies Norway and Iceland. In 2003, the Chinese
government opened the Arctic Yellow River Station on the Norwegian
island of Svalbard and has built on this presence recently with the
October 2018 opening of the China-Iceland Arctic Science Observatory
(CIAO)---a permanent scientific installation dedicated to a wide
spectrum of research objectives, including geomagnetic field
measurements, aurora observations, climatology, glaciology,
oceanography, and biology.

#### Rare earth extraction unleashes irreversible destruction\-\--Open-pit operations pollute coastlines, torch fuel, and produce mountains of toxic waste that outlast generations.

**Kamis '22** \[James; September 1; Geologist and AAPG member of 41
years, BS in Geology from Northern Illinois University, MS in Geology
from Idaho State University, former worker for Becker Industries, ARCO,
Cross Timbers, Texaco, Fina, Union 76, and BTA Oil Producers; Climate
Change Dispatch, "The Dirty Truth About Open-Pit Mining For Greenland's
Rare Earth Minerals,"
climatechangedispatch.com/the-dirty-truth-about-open-pit-mining-for-greenlands-rare-earth-minerals\]
//jwh

Except **[open-pit mining of Greenland's rare earth metals will have a
far greater impact environmental impact than portrayed by the
media]{.underline}**, the United States Government, and mining
companies.

I was one of two onsite geologists who aided in the expansion of Idaho's
huge open-pit Maybe Canyon Phosphate Mine. This experience taught me
that it is difficult to appreciate the size and complex operations of a
giant open-pit mine unless you have worked in one.

I am certain the public does not have any idea of the processes involved
in open-pit mining. Like most products, the public thinks that rare
earth metals magically appear.

They Don't.

The photo below is Utah's Kennicott open-pit copper mine, which is 2.5
miles wide, 0.75 miles deep, and has removed six billion tons of rock
rich in copper.

Copper is a rare earth metal so this mine is an excellent example of
what an open-pit mine in Greenland would look like.

[Operations]{.underline} of an open-pit mine

[Use **chemicals**]{.underline} that were taken from another mined area
to explode solid rock areas of the mine.

Use [**diesel**-powered machines]{.underline} to reduce the size of
large boulders created by the explosions.

Utilize **[multiple monster diesel-fueled trucks]{.underline}** to haul
off the crushed rock. Trucks that are built with iron extracted from
another mine and rubber tires that are [generated by **processing
oil**]{.underline}. You need to drill wells, some in ocean areas, to
produce oil.

[Build a **diesel**]{.underline}- [or **natural gas**-fueled **refinery
plant**]{.underline}. It is important to note that natural gas is
composed of 90% methane. So, the [refineries]{.underline} will [**emit
huge** amounts of **methane** into the atmosphere]{.underline}. Also, [a
rare earth metal plant needs **20 tons of water**]{.underline} (150
trillion gallons of water) [to make **one ton** of rare earth
metals]{.underline}.

Build a large reservoir capable of retaining vast amounts of mined waste
rock.

[Construct a network of **huge pipelines** to transport **mined waste
rock** to the]{.underline} mined waste-rock retaining
[reservoir]{.underline}.

[Ship the]{.underline} now [**concentrated metals** to another
processing plant]{.underline} that prepares the metals, which are placed
in a renewable-energy device.

[Restore the mined area to its **original** state]{.underline}. [This
process uses **monster trucks**]{.underline}, graders, etc. So, the
**[restoration]{.underline}** process [also has a **large carbon
footprint**]{.underline}.

**Kamis '22** \[James; September 1; Geologist and AAPG member of 41

4\. [Mercury Pollution]{.underline}

Research studies have proven that the rivers, ocean bays, and open ocean
waters [in the **proposed** Greenland mining areas have **extremely high
concentrations** of natural mercury]{.underline}.

Mercury comes from bedrock layers that are mercury rich. Mercury is a
rare earth mineral. [If anything goes **awry** with the **mining**
process]{.underline}, [**huge** additional **amounts** of]{.underline}
natural [mercury will get **dumped** into Greenland's **seawater** and
**freshwater** systems]{.underline}.

"Unlike polluted rivers in other parts of the world, contaminated by
industrial activity, the researchers believe the Greenland mercury is
coming from natural sources. If it were coming from human pollution,
then the snow on top of the ice sheet should also be full of
mercury---yet previous studies have shown it's comparatively clean.
Instead, the [scientists believe]{.underline} the **[meltwater
mercury]{.underline}** is probably leaching out of the bedrock beneath
the ice. Mercury concentration in the meltwater rivers was at least [an
**order of magnitude higher** than the concentrations found in
**ordinary** rivers across the Arctic]{.underline}. These concentrations
became slightly diluted by the time they flowed out into the
fjords---but were still higher than expected, the researchers say. [Even
after **mingling** with the **salty water**]{.underline}, [the
**levels**]{.underline} in the fjords **[remained]{.underline}** about
[an **order of magnitude higher** than]{.underline} the mercury levels
[found in **most** open ocean **waters**]{.underline}" (see here).

#### Toxic leaching, groundwater contamination, and sterile soil from decades of mining show that "clean extraction" is a myth\-\--Greenland would inherit the same poisoned legacy.

**Earth '20** \[Earth.org; July 14; Environmental news platform,
credible independent journalism outlet, nonprofit organization committed
to transparency, based in Hong Kong with a global perspective, focused
on climate science and sustainability, publishes rigorously researched
articles, collaborates with scientists and policy experts, trusted
source for ecological analysis, aims to inform public discourse and
influence environmental policy; Earth.org, "How Rare-Earth Mining Has
Devastated China's Environment,"
earth.org/rare-earth-mining-has-devastated-chinas-environment\] //jwh

[It's **challenging** to mine]{.underline} and process [rare earths
**without** harming the environment]{.underline}. The problems are
related to the two primary extraction methods.

The first involves [**removing** the topsoil]{.underline},
[**transporting** it to a **leaching** pond]{.underline}, [and adding
**chemicals**]{.underline} (such as ammonium sulfate and ammonium
chloride) to separate out the metals. The chemicals used in this
separation process can [create **air pollution**]{.underline}, [cause
**erosion**]{.underline}, [and **leach** into groundwater]{.underline}.

The second processing method [involves **drilling** holes into the
ground]{.underline}, inserting [**PVC** pipes and **rubber** hoses and
**pumping** chemicals]{.underline} to flush out earth. The resulting
[**slurry** is]{.underline} then [pumped into **leaching**
ponds]{.underline} to separate out the rare-earth metal.

This method creates the same problems as with topsoil removal with the
addition of the PVC pipes, rubber hoses and other sundry used by mining
crews remaining littered in the mines.

[Abandoned mines pose **ongoing** environmental
**hazards**]{.underline}. [Remaining chemicals]{.underline} can continue
to [**leach** into]{.underline} groundwater.

Mines may also contain pools of wastewater that can potentially [pour
into **local waterways**]{.underline}. For example, in its 2016 report,
China Water Risk highlighted an abandoned mine in Ganzhou where
untreated chemicals flow from leaching ponds when it rains.

Problems in China

[The **pollution** resulting]{.underline} from rare-earth mining has
[created soil **incapable** of supporting **crops** and water supplies
have been **contaminated**]{.underline}.

Chinese officials have attempted to counteract these threats by shutting
down a large number of mines, especially the smaller and the illegal
ones, but there are still severe, large-scale threats that remain
unresolved.

Clean-Up Efforts

From north near the Mongolian border to south in Guangdong, China is
[**struggling** to **clean-up** the environment polluted by
**mining**]{.underline} and some claim they are [making things
**worse**]{.underline}. [The clean-up process is
**expensive**]{.underline} and time-consuming, and some say [it could be
**50-100 years** for the environment to recover]{.underline}.

#### Greenland ore grades are too low\-\--Mining would require massive pits and unsustainable energy use.

**Christiansen '25** \[Flemming; May 8; Mining and geology consultant at

Nor do Greenland's deposits fare well in comparison with other mines or

or attractive deposits [are between **4** and **8** percent]{.underline}
rare earths. [The **ore** at the mines at]{.underline} Mount Weld in
[**Australia** and]{.underline} Mountain Pass in
**[California]{.underline}**, [and]{.underline} the deposits at
**[Nolans Bore]{.underline}** in Australia [and **Bear

#### Harsh Arctic terrain dooms mining\-\--Greenland's conditions repel investment and scale.

**Rasmussen '25** \[Sune; March 4; Correspondent who covers Afghanistan,
Iran and North European affairs for The Wall Street Journal, reporting
on the fall of Kabul in 2021 was nominated for a Pulitzer Prize, 2019
book in Danish on the Afghan war was shortlisted for a Cavling Prize,

//jwh

[Mining]{.underline} companies [in Greenland]{.underline} operate in one
of [the **most challenging** environments in the **world** because of
the]{.underline} Danish territory's [**sparse**
infrastructure]{.underline}, [**hostile** weather and a **tricky**
political **climate**]{.underline}. [Mining]{.underline} here [is
**expensive**]{.underline}, [and **few** investors are **willing** to
pay for it]{.underline} given the uncertainties.

Despite its extraordinary mineral wealth, [Greenland has **only two**
active mines]{.underline}: a gold mine in commissioning phase and a mine
producing anorthosite, used in fiberglass, paints and other construction
materials. [A ruby mine **closed** last year owing to
**bankruptcy**]{.underline}. Greenland has extended around 100 mining
licenses, mostly for exploration.

"[Investing in Greenland is **not for the faint of
heart**]{.underline}," said Brian Hanrahan, chief executive officer of
Lumina Sustainable Materials, which operates the anorthosite mine on the
west coast of Greenland. "[The local logistics are **incredibly
complex**]{.underline}."

Building a mine here [involves **high** startup **costs**]{.underline},
[and has to be done from **scratch** in **rugged terrain**]{.underline}.
Greenland is nearly one-fourth the size of the U.S., and about [**80%**
of it is covered by ice]{.underline}, [with **deep fjords** and **ice**
sheets up to a **mile thick**]{.underline}. [There are **no roads**
between **settlements**]{.underline}, [and **shipping** is
**treacherous** because of **floating ice** off the coast]{.underline}.

[Bureaucracy is a **hindrance**]{.underline}, too. The [process of
**granting licenses** to **foreign** companies to mine is **lengthy**
and **cumbersome**]{.underline}. While applying for licenses, companies
need to keep employees on payroll with benefits. With a population of
57,000, Greenland's labor market is tight.

#### REE mining unleashes toxic and radioactive pollution\-\--Greenland lacks safeguards.

**Gregory '23** \[Madeleine; April 23; Print editor at Atmos, work
focuses on ecology, environmental justice, and climate change, former
writer for the Vice and Sierra Magazine; Atmos, "Greenland Has Climate
Change-Fighting Minerals. Will it Mine Them?,"
atmos.earth/greenland-rare-earth-elements-glaciers\] //jwh

[**Rare** earth elements]{.underline} are not rare exactly. Rather, they
[exist in **small quantities** in the natural world]{.underline}, so
it's hard to find concentrations worth mining. [When you do find usable
deposits]{.underline}, in minerals such as bastnaesite and monazite,
[these minerals require **heavy processing** to get **usable**
REEs]{.underline}.

Meanwhile, in the human world, they can be found everywhere. The glassy
black display of every smartphone likely contains praseodymium and
dysprosium. The magnets tucked in the microphone and speaker contain
praseodymium and neodymium. The magnets in the motors of electric cars
have praseodymium, neodymium, and dysprosium. So do the magnets in wind
turbines. These three elements are all classified as rare earth
elements, and all of them are key to the transition away from fossil
fuels.

[Every step]{.underline}, [from **extraction** to
**processing**]{.underline}, [poses **threats** to the]{.underline}
surrounding [environment]{.underline} in the absence of tight,
rigorously enforced environmental regulations. Balaram Vysetti, chief
scientist at the National Geophysical Research Institute in Hyderabad,
India, said in an email that the "**[cutting]{.underline}**,
**[drilling]{.underline}**, **[blasting]{.underline}**,
**[transportation]{.underline}**, **[stockpiling]{.underline}**, [and
**processing**]{.underline}" of REEs can [release **dust** containing
REEs and]{.underline} other [**toxic metals** into the air]{.underline},
[impacting **human** and **ecosystem** health]{.underline}. If the
deposit contains radioactive elements like **[uranium]{.underline}** or
**[thorium]{.underline}**, Vysetti said that the [**radioactivity** can
contaminate **air**]{.underline}, **[water]{.underline}**,
**[soil]{.underline}**, [and **groundwater**]{.underline}.

#### Greenland mining is a climate timebomb\-\--Melts ice, spreads toxins, and destabilizes infrastructure.

**Simon '25** \[Matt; March 26;S enior staff writer covering biology,
robotics, and the environment for WIRED, author of A Poison Like No
Other: How Microplastics Corrupted Our Planet and Our Bodies; WIRED,
"Beneath Greenland's Ice Lies a Climate Solution---and a New
Geopolitical Battleground,"
wired.com/story/beneath-greenlands-ice-lies-a-climate-solution-and-a-new-geopolitical-battleground\]
//jwh

[**Dramatic** change on]{.underline} and around [the
island]{.underline}, though, could [make mining for rare earths **even
more complicated**]{.underline}. While the loss of floating ice in the
waters around the island makes it easier and safer for ships to
navigate, [more **chunks** of glaciers will **drop** into the ocean as
the world warms]{.underline}, which could [become **especially
hazardous** for ships]{.underline}, à la the Titanic.

Even given the rapid loss of Greenland's 650,000-square-mile ice sheet,
though, it would take a long while to lose it all---it's 1.4 miles thick
on average. The Earth itself is also frozen in parts of the island,
known as permafrost, which will thaw in the nearer term as temperatures
rise. "[That\'s going to give you certainly **instability** in terms of
building **access** roads]{.underline} and such," said Paul Bierman, a
geologist at the University of Vermont and author of the book When the
Ice Is Gone: What a Greenland Ice Core Reveals About Earth\'s Tumultuous
History and Perilous Future. "The climate is changing, so I think [it\'s
going to be a **very dynamic** environment in which to **extract**
minerals]{.underline}."

[Mining pollution]{.underline}, too, [is a **major
concern**]{.underline}: [The **accessible** land along the island's
**ice-free** edges is]{.underline} also [where **humans**
live]{.underline}. [As **mining** equipment and **ships** burn **fossil
fuels**]{.underline}, [they produce **black carbon**]{.underline}. When
this settles on ice, [it **darkens** the surface]{.underline},
[which]{.underline} then [**absorbs** more
**sunlight**]{.underline}---think of how much hotter you get wearing a
black shirt than a white shirt on a summer day. This could [**further
accelerate** the **melting** of Greenland's **precarious** ice
sheet]{.underline}. A 2022 study also found that three legacy mines in
Greenland heavily polluted the local environment with metals, like lead
and zinc, due to the lack of environmental studies and regulation prior
to the 1970s. But it also found no significant pollution at mines
established in the last 20 years.

A more immediate problem with mining is [the]{.underline} potentially
[**toxic dust** generated by so much **machinery**]{.underline}, said
Niels Henrik Hooge, a campaigner at NOAH, the Danish chapter of the
environmental organization Friends of the Earth. "That\'s [a
**concern**]{.underline}, [because all the **mining projects** are
located in areas where **people** live]{.underline}, or potentially
could live," Hooge said. "Everything is a bit different in the Arctic,
because **[the environment does not recover very quickly when
polluted]{.underline}**."

**CCI '25** \[Conservation Core India; March 31; Non-profit Conservation

#### Great power mineral rush hijacks Greenlandic sovereignty and shreds Arctic protections.

**Hong '25** \[Nong; June 2; Executive Director & Senior Fellow at The
Institute for China-America Studies, Head of the Maritime Affairs

[Greenland's **mining** politics encapsulate **broader** Arctic

economic **[footprints]{.underline}**, [the **island** becomes a **proxy
arena** for **strategic competition**]{.underline}. US-China rivalry is

normative power pushing environmental safeguards.

This dynamic raises two concerns. First, [Greenland]{.underline} may
become over-politicised. As its autonomy within the Kingdom of Denmark
grows, Nuuk seeks to chart its own economic path. Yet external pressure
risks reducing Greenlandic agency, especially if caught in US-China
tension.

ice **already** symbolises global **climate change**]{.underline}.
Without stringent safeguards, [mining expansion]{.underline} could
[threaten **fragile ecosystems** and **Indigenous**

# Greenland Secession DA

### 1NC

#### Support for independence is [growing]{.underline}, but economic concerns [deter]{.underline} full autonomy now. Western mining in the region [guarantees secession]{.underline}.

Reagan **Patrowicz 25**, MA in Geopolitics, Resources and Territory at
King's College London, 3/11/2025, "Greenland's opportunity to capitalise
on growing international interest,\"
https://menas.co.uk/blog/greenlands-opportunity-to-capitalise-on-growing-international-interest

A Path to Independence?

It is no secret that [[many]{.mark} in Greenland [**support**
independence]{.mark}]{.underline}. A 2025 poll revealed that
[approximately 84% of its population **wants it** [but only **45%** do
if it does **not negatively impact**]{.mark} their [standard of
living]{.mark}]{.underline}. In a 2024 survey, [[Greenlanders
identified]{.mark} 'higher [living costs']{.mark}]{.underline} and the
'economic situation' [[as]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[most pressing
challenges]{.mark} facing]{.underline} their [society]{.underline}.
Given these concerns, the potential [[**economic risks** associated with
independence have led to **caution**]{.underline}]{.mark} that the
standard of living could be jeopardised.

[[Greenland is]{.underline}]{.mark} currently [financially [dependent
on]{.mark}]{.underline} an annual block grant from
**[[Denmark]{.underline}]{.mark}** which accounts for more than half of
its parliamentary budget. The [economic situation has been]{.underline}
the [[primary factor inhibiting]{.underline}]{.mark} the indigenous
[[independence]{.mark} movement]{.underline}. The increasing **[[Western
interest]{.underline}]{.mark}** in Greenland
[[could]{.underline}]{.mark}, however, [[provide]{.mark} it with
a]{.underline} viable **[[path to independence]{.underline}]{.mark}**.
Although some locals are understandably cautious about foreign
interests, there is significant support for more cooperation [with the
**US**]{.underline}, Canada, Denmark and the EU.

[Greenland's 'open for business' approach]{.underline} with the West
[could provide mutually beneficial outcomes]{.underline}, and
particularly through the promotion of its mining and energy
opportunities. [[An increase in]{.underline}]{.mark} cooperation and
[[**investment** could drive]{.underline}]{.mark} the [sufficient
economic [**development** and stability needed to support]{.mark}
Greenlandic [society, making independence a **clear option**]{.mark}
while limiting risks that would sacrifice]{.underline} the [Greenlandic
standard of living]{.underline}.

The March 2025 Election

Greenland's 11 March election is arguably its most important and will
garner unprecedented international attention. The key election issues
include:

Independence -- most Greenlandic political parties support independence
but their views differ on the best strategy and timeline;

Partnership with foreign countries -- Greenland's choice of who to
cooperate with, and to what extent, differ amongst the parties;

Resource Governance -- varying opinions on the management of resource
wealth and particularly balancing the mining permitting policies with
environmental concerns. The current ban on uranium mining ban is a key
example of these differences.

A January 2025 poll showed a lead of approximately 9% for in incumbent
Inuit Ataqatigiit party. However, with the Siumut party's promise of a
post-election independence referendum, and the Naleraq party's growing
popularity, it is difficult to accurately predict the results. The
election results are expected in the early on 12 March and could shape
Greenland's geopolitical and economic future.

Greenland's growing international relevance presents both opportunities
and challenges as it navigates its future. While the prospect of a US
purchase remains unrealistic, the increasing Western engagement ---
particularly in mining, energy and security --- offers potential
economic benefits that could support Greenland's independence goals.
Today's election will be a pivotal moment, determining the island's
approach to foreign partnerships and its path toward greater
self-sufficiency.

UPDATE: 12 March [[2025 election]{.underline}]{.mark} results

[Although]{.underline} the [polls predicted]{.underline} that [the
incumbent party would retain power]{.underline}, the [opposition
performed]{.underline} unexpectedly [**well**. The Demokraatit party
secured a surprise victory]{.underline} by winning the largest share of
votes for the first time in its history. Since the 2021 election it
gained over 20% and its seat count jumped from three to ten in the
31-seat parliament. Another opposition party, Naleraq, also performed
strongly and came second. [This [result is
**unprecedented**]{.mark}]{.underline}, because either the Inuit
Ataqatigiit or Siumut have won every election since 1991. [The
[Greenlandic people **clearly wanted a change**]{.mark}]{.underline} of
government and direction.

[Although all four parties **support** independence, they differ in
their approach. Demokraatit favours a gradual path to
independence]{.underline}, with an in initial focus on building a strong
economic and social foundation first. [Both opposition parties support
increasing]{.underline} economic [cooperation with Western allies, which

[self-sufficiency]{.underline} goals. The [[**coming days** will be
