#### Independence upsets u[parity]{.underline} in the Arctic, prompting nuclear war.

John **Ash 22**, Associate at the Scott Polar Research
Institute, University of Cambridge, 2022, "An Arctic Promised Land:
Greenlandic Independence and Security," Policy Studies Yearbook, Vol.
12, Issue 1, pp. 167-213, https://doi.org/10.18278/psy.12.1.7

This article is concerned with military security in the Arctic region
and the policy options surrounding it. The knowledge gap it addresses is
in two parts: first, how Greenland as a nascent Arctic state could
properly discharge the responsibility of defending itself and its
population. Second, the [prospective [effects of **Greenlandic
independence** on]{.mark} Arctic **[geopolitical
stability]{.mark}**]{.underline}. It is not directed at any specific
stakeholder group and has no comment to make regarding the moral rights
to self-determination of a population---indigenous or otherwise---that
identifies itself as a distinct community. Nor does it address any
aspect of perceived or actual historical wrongdoing by any state party.
Inevitably defense issues, and how they are to be addressed, will form a
part of the governmental considerations of any new nation state,
including the likely response by other nations.

Despite the formal cessation of the Cold War, the [balance of
geopolitical forces that exercise sovereignty over]{.underline} the
[Arctic remains **extant**]{.underline}.1 In effect, the Arctic is a
mediterranean ocean across which Russia confronts four NATO nations. Due
to its geographical location, Greenland forms a key element in that
balance---a wedge in one of the power blocs---which makes it an
excellent case, pertinent to Policy Studies Yearbook readers, for
studying the intersection of several recent and current policy issues
well beyond defense and security concerns. Climate change has brought
the prospect of [[increased availability of]{.mark} Arctic natural
[resources]{.mark}]{.underline}, and with it, concerns that these [might
[provide focal points for **conflict**]{.mark}]{.underline} (Ash 2016).
To date, these fears have proven groundless and the latest assessment by
the U.S. National Intelligence Council (U.S. NIC 2021, 8) anticipates a
regional increase in competition, largely economic in nature, with
[a]{.underline} modest [[increase]{.mark} in the [risk of
**miscalculation**]{.mark}]{.underline} by 2040. What has not abated is
the [[sensitivity in Russia concerning]{.underline}]{.mark} the [nuclear
**[second-strike capability]{.mark}** of its Northern Fleet
submarines]{.underline} based on the Kola Peninsula (Boulègue 2019,
6-8). [[In a]{.mark} future]{.underline} major armed
[**[conflict]{.mark}**, air, maritime, and land assets would contest
control of]{.underline} critical circum-[Arctic regions]{.underline}. If
the situation escalated, **[[nuclear missiles]{.underline}]{.mark}**
from both sides [[would cross]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[Arctic]{.mark}
on ballistic trajectories]{.underline}. Consequently, [[**close
attention** is paid to]{.underline}]{.mark} any increment or reduction
in [regional military capability]{.underline} (see e.g., Poulin 2016),
[and]{.underline} the [continued [**presence**, or]{.mark}]{.underline}
future [[**absence** of Greenland as a **sovereign polity**]{.mark}
from]{.underline} the [balance of power [would
influence]{.mark}]{.underline} the [political [symmetry that underpins
current **stability**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Shi (2019 and citations therein) identifies four principal
[[**incentives** behind]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[demand for
Greenlandic independence]{.underline}]{.mark}. First, a long-term growth
in the confidence of Greenlandic people with respect to the management
of domestic and foreign affairs. Citing Beukel, Jensen, and Rytter (2010
30), Shi notes the Second World War (WWII) as a turning point in this
regard, as it brought contact with external actors other than Danes.
Second, [[increased prosperity in a more
**diversified**]{.underline}]{.mark} and efficient
**[[economy]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Third, a long-term dissatisfaction
with Denmark at an emotional level, relating to the colonial past. And
finally, the sense of identity among indigenous Greenlandic people and a
related growing national identity. [However]{.underline}, the
[[desire]{.mark} for independence [is **nuanced**. While a
poll]{.mark}]{.underline} in [2016 [indicated a]{.mark} 64-percent
[majority]{.mark} among]{.underline} the [Greenlandic people [for full
autonomy]{.mark}]{.underline} (Skydsbjerg and Turnowsky 2016), [a 2017
[survey revealed]{.mark} a 78-percent [opposition if it entailed]{.mark}
a [fall in living standards]{.mark}]{.underline} (Bjerregaard 2017).

The [political process leading toward independence has been
protracted]{.underline}. A 1953 change to the Danish constitution
incorporated Greenland into Denmark as a province, conferring
parliamentary representation (constituteproject.org 2021). Home rule was
granted in 1979 with defense, together with foreign affairs, currency,
and the legal system remaining under the jurisdiction of Denmark.2
Following a 2008 referendum (Göcke 2009), a 2009 self-rule law granted
the Greenlandic government (Naalakkersuisut) extensive rights, including
autonomy with respect to the legal system, law enforcement, the Coast
Guard, and foreign affairs in matters relating exclusively to Greenland
and those responsibilities transferred under the Act.3 The powers
granted to Naalakkersuisut did not limit the Danish authorities'
constitutional responsibility and powers in foreign and security policy.
However, the [Act authorized [Greenland]{.mark} to **declare** full
[independence, subject to approval by]{.mark} a **[referendum]{.mark}**
of the Greenlandic people]{.underline}.

[[Despite **reservations**]{.mark} among]{.underline} the [Greenlandic
population]{.underline}, the [[drive toward independence **has**
momentum]{.underline}]{.mark}, and the question of how the Greenland
government would exercise sovereign authority with respect to defense is
now becoming urgent. Greenland, together with the Faroe Islands, is
assuming greater authority in foreign affairs and defense, having
recently signed terms of reference with Denmark for the creation of a
special contact committee to discuss matters of national and common
interests (Danilov 2021). This agreement comes against the backdrop of a
cabinet reshuffle in the Greenland government, in which the premier took
personal charge of foreign affairs, relieving the foreign minister of
those responsibilities (McGwin 2021). The action follows publication of
a news article in which the minister was reported as questioning whether
non-Inuit individuals should be permitted to participate in a vote on
Greenland's independence (McGwin 2021). While the minister regarded the
article as misinterpreting his comments (McGwin 2021), the premier's
response underlines the sensitivity and currency of the independence
question. At the same time, a general increase in international tension
is reflected in perceptions of the Arctic as a potential future
battlespace. As Faroe Islands' Prime Minister Bárður á Steig Nielsen
observed: "The North Atlantic and the Arctic are increasingly in the
spotlight of security policy, and therefore the active participation of
the Faroe Islands and Greenland is also of growing importance" (cited in
Danilov 2021).

## Impact

### Impact\-\-\--2NC

#### Secession leads to quick Arctic conflict.

**Banerjee '24** \[Dhruv; March 24; Political writer approaching
contemporary politics and International Relations for Sciences Po Paris;
Modern Diplomacy, "How Greenland's Push for Independence Could Trigger
an Unprecedented Global Race for Influence,"
moderndiplomacy.eu/2024/03/24/how-greenlands-push-for-independence-could-trigger-an-unprecedented-global-race-for-influence\]
//jwh

The European Union's recent [[move to **coax Greenland** into]{.mark}
increased **economic cooperation**]{.underline}, particularly [in terms
of **[resource extraction]{.mark}**, is]{.underline} [a thinly [veiled
attempt to make **inroads** into]{.mark}]{.underline} what is set to be
the stage for a [compelling [geopolitical
**struggle**]{.mark}]{.underline}. There is a [**hidden** element behind
this]{.underline} seemingly straightforward [economic move: Greenland's
potential **autonomy** in the future also [holds **massive security
implications**]{.mark}. The]{.underline} small population of 65,000
residing on the [world's **largest** island]{.underline} has
[over]{.underline} the past few years [advocated for **independence**
from Denmark]{.underline}. However, what seems to be a relatively
localised issue actually [hides **simmering** [international
**tensions**]{.mark}]{.underline}, which could likely [[cause a rat
race]{.mark} among global powers [for increased **influence** over
Greenland]{.mark}]{.underline}. After all, it [contains]{.underline} the
[**resplendent** lure of **vast untapped** resources]{.underline} while
also [serving as a potential gateway to the Arctic]{.underline}.

The Rocky Relationship between Greenland and Denmark

The [**crux** of the issue]{.underline} really [began about a
**millennium** ago]{.underline}. The story [goes that an Icelandic
delinquent, **Eriksson**]{.underline} the Red, was [sent to the island
in **exile**]{.underline}. Realising the vast swathes of empty space
(warmer at the time) had potential, he [**circulated** this information
back **home**]{.underline}. Armed with curiosity, the [ever-enthusiastic
Norsemen began sending **expeditions**]{.underline}. A slew of
settlements came up, many religiously influenced by Norway's adherence
to the church, eventually [setting **Christianity** on a **collision
course** with the population]{.underline} that had expanded towards the
north of the island---[the **Inuit**]{.underline}. Although the Viking
presence on the island would fizzle out, these endeavours were
eventually cited as historical justification for [Denmark's **colonial**
project]{.underline} in the 18th century. [Funded by the
**king**]{.underline}, the Alchemist-Pastor Hans Egede had
[undertaken]{.underline} a [mission to spread **Lutheran** beliefs
across Greenland]{.underline}. In reality, this [served as a
**smokescreen** for Danish merchants to fill their **pockets** with the
vast natural resources]{.underline} that lay ripe for the taking [on the
island]{.underline}. With time, this two-fold approach became more
evident, as the monopolisation of trade on the island by the Royal
Greenland Trading Department in 1776 was synchronous with an increased
push for Christianization of the Inuits. Indeed, so goes the age-old
colonial strategy of matching economic exploitation with claims of
"civilising" native populations. Even after World War 2, subtle forms of
colonial dominance persisted, with accusations that Danish elites
fuelled a belief in the exiguous agency of the island's residents.

However, the [[**discourse** regarding Greenland]{.mark}'s own
**culture** and **[autonomy]{.mark}**]{.underline} inadvertently took
stead towards the end of the 20th century, [**culminating** in the
[]{.mark}**devolution** of certain functions]{.underline} away from the
Danish government, resulting in "home rule status" being provided in
1979. The following decades saw an [increased push to **galvanise** upon
this]{.underline}, with the 2009 "Act on Greenland Self Government"
essentially [placing most **tenets** of **economics** and **welfare** in
the hands of the regional government]{.underline}, with Copenhagen
desperately grasping onto decisions relating to security and foreign
policy. The [gradual **unravelling** of this process]{.underline}
resulted in the 2023 draft constitution, meant [to **spearhead**
Greenland into a new **era** of independence. An era signifying
**cultural** and **economic** autonomy]{.underline}, with a
parliamentary republic and no Danish monarchy looming over the 90
percent Inuit population. **[If only it were so simple]{.underline}**

The Complicated Question of Independence

If Greenland were to go ahead and push for complete secession, this
would by no means be a smooth-sailing process. For starters, the glaring
elephant in the room is the fact that the Danish Government annually
provides a grant of approximately half a billion euros to Greenland, a
whopping sum covering over half of Greenland's public budget. Cutting
this off overnight would be catastrophic in a similar vein to
post-Soviet shock therapy. The transition has to be a phased one, and
even then, fine-tuning the details will be logistically cumbersome.

The search for alternative sources to make the economy tick does yield
some options, but these all have limitations. Growing the fishing
industry, which has been a staple for them throughout history, is a
lucrative but insufficient option. People are used to a high standard of
living, and one of the preconditions for secession has been to ensure no
reduction from this standard. Current domestic industries will likely be
insufficient to meet this expectation. This is exacerbated by a lack of
infrastructural development, with railroads, roads, and other forms of
transportation significantly lacking. Their marked remoteness and lack
of self-sufficiency would not help this situation and would eventually
make them excessively dependent on imports. What can, however, fulfil
these gaping deficiencies is the subject of much contention: mining and
extraction.

As the effects of global warming tear through the island, reducing ice
cover by approximately 100 billion metric tonnes annually, the ground
yields new evidence of vast mineral deposits. [Like **moths** to
**flames**, major **corporations** have **swarmed** towards [the
prospect of fresh sources to **exploit**]{.mark}]{.underline}. While
this would definitely be a way out for Greenland from an economic
perspective, this remains a rather unpopular proposition. The last
elections in 2021 raged primarily around the issue of rare earth and
uranium mining in Kvanefjeld (under an Australian firm). Allegations
that projects such as these would [[cause]{.mark} **significant**
environmental **degradation**]{.underline} and would [**incur** the
**risk** of **radioactive** exposure]{.underline}. While striking a
balance between economic interests and natural conservation could be a
way forward, the staunchly anti-mining coalition government renders
legislation that strikes a genuine compromise unlikely. Thus, mining
also does not seem like an immediately feasible solution, which brings
us to more political considerations... Can Greenland turn to others for
help?

Greenland's potential role in the [[**competition** to **militarise**
the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}

The 2023 draft constitution contained a free association, essentially
signalling that an independent Greenland could initially place some
degree of dependence on some external actor. Such a proposition is
attractive for many.

In 2019, there was an odd controversy when Donald Trump admitted that
the USA was considering buying Greenland, likening it to a real estate
sale. While this is not exactly a feasible venture given the importance
of sovereignty in contemporary international law, there is nonetheless
some interesting information to be extracted from it. [[If
Greenland]{.mark} were to [become **independent**, the USA would]{.mark}
more than likely [come **knocking** at their **door**]{.mark} for
increased strategic **cooperation**]{.underline}. They would not even be
starting from scratch, given that there are existing American military
installations, [including the large **Thule air base**]{.underline}
present on the northwestern edge of the island. The reason behind
Greenland's importance is simple. The [**melting Arctic** can be [easily
**militarised** through]{.mark} access from [the
island]{.mark}]{.underline}. New polar routes would pit Russia and the
USA in uncomfortable proximity, making it essential for both sides to
make up ground quickly.

At the same time, [**neglecting** China]{.underline} from this
discussion [would also be **premature**]{.underline}, despite its
physical distance. Hypothetically, [if [an independent
Greenland]{.mark}]{.underline} opted not to join NATO, [this [would
provide **China** and **Russia**]{.mark} with the **impetus** [to make
headway into **influencing** their economies and
**policies**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Confronted with an opportunity to
make the first move in consolidating control over the northern sea
route, as well as the vast oil and energy resources along it
(potentially 10--30 percent of the world's undiscovered reserves),
[[**neither** major power]{.mark} would be [likely to **hold
back**]{.mark}]{.underline}. In particular, [being able to **implement**
the polar sea rout]{.underline}, would [[prove]{.underline}]{.mark} to
be [[**extremely handy** for China's economic
**expansion**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Thus goes the [tale of [a **brewing competition** between major
powers]{.mark}]{.underline}, set to [[take place]{.mark} in a region
that could **prove** [to be the most **prominent**]{.mark} [geopolitical
**arena**]{.mark}]{.underline} of the coming decades, [with
**questions** of **autonomy**]{.underline}, military, trade,
exploitation, and secession all melding together. At the centre of it
all lies peaceful, serene Greenland.

### Impact\-\--Great Power War\-\--2NC

#### Only great power war goes nuclear.

**Brands '19** \[Hal and Charles Edel; November 18; Distinguished
Professor of Global Affairs at the Johns Hopkins University School of
Advanced International Studies; National Defense University Press, "The
Lessons of Tragedy," 978-0300238242\]

Yet the [**failings** and [**frustrations** of U.S. **strategy**
were]{.mark} **certainly** [**no greater** than]{.mark} those
**encountered** in [**Vietnam**, **Korea**, or other **Cold War**
episodes]{.mark}, and [America's]{.mark} inevitable [**post--Cold War**
travails]{.mark} often [**obscured** how much U.S. **statecraft**
accomplished]{.mark}]{.underline}. It was not inevitable that the
post--Cold War environment would prove to be as relatively benign as it
was. Quite the opposite: in the early 1990s, some [leading
**international relations** scholars]{.underline} believed that the
world was [entering a **new era** of **vicious
competition**]{.underline} and instability. "**[We will soon miss the
Cold War]{.underline}**," John Mearsheimer warned; rampant nuclear
proliferation, geopolitical revisionism, and even [**great-power
crises** and wars were **likely**]{.underline} as the [**end** of
**bipolarity** unleashed **international** demons]{.underline} that had
been pent up for forty-five years. Yet [[for all the ills]{.mark} that
afflicted the post-Cold war world]{.underline}---humanitarian crises,
regional conflicts, and catastrophic terrorism, among others---[[what is
**highlighted**]{.mark} stands out [is the role of **continued**
American **engagement** in keeping]{.mark} the more [**positive** trends
going and **stifling**]{.mark} the sources of greater **[geopolitical
upheaval]{.mark}**]{.underline}. As William Wohlforth has noted,
[[American primacy]{.mark} and activism [acted as a]{.mark} powerful
[**deterrent** to **great-power conflict** by creating]{.mark} enormous
[**disincentives** for **Russia**, **China**]{.mark}, or other actors
[to **incur**]{.mark} the "[focused **enmity**]{.mark}" of the United
States. The persistence and even [extension of]{.mark} the [U.S.
**security blanket** smothered **potential instability**]{.mark} in
unsettled regions]{.underline} such as Eastern Europe, while removing
any possibility of German or Japanese revanchism---a prospect much
feared in the early 1990s---by keeping those countries tightly lashed to
Washington. [American [**intervention** helped **extinguish** bloody
**conflicts** in the **Balkans** **before** they could **spread** to
neighboring countries; U.S.]{.mark} diplomatic and **military**
[pressure kept **aggressive tyrannies**]{.mark} such as **Iraq**,
**Iran**, and **North Korea** [**bottled** up and helped **slow** the
**spread** of nuclear weapons]{.mark}]{.underline}. U.S. support helped
[democratic forces **triumph**]{.underline} in countries from Haiti to
Poland, as the [**number** of democracies **rose**]{.underline} from 76
in 1990 to 120 in 2000; America [**crucially assisted** the **advance**
of globalization]{.underline} and the **[broad prosperity]{.underline}**
that came with it by promoting pro-market policies and providing the
[**necessary** climate of **reassurance** and
**stability**]{.underline}.

#### Great power war causes extinction\-\--It's a threat multiplier.

**Clare '25** \[Stephen; Updated in March; Research Fellow at the
Forethought Foundation for Global Priorities Research, B.A. from
McMaster University, M.A. from McGill University; 80,000 Hours, "Great
power conflict," 80000hours.org/problem-profiles/great-power-conflict\]
//jwh

At some point in the [21st century, [an **unwinnable war** may be
**fought**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[[A **modern** great power war]{.mark} could [see **nuclear
weapons**, **bioweapons**, **autonomous weapons**, and other
**destructive** **new tech**nologies deployed]{.mark} on
an [**unprecedented** scale]{.mark}]{.underline}.

It would probably be the [**most destructive** event]{.underline} in
history, [[**shattering** our world]{.underline}]{.mark}. It could
[even [**threaten** us with **extinction**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

We've come [**perilously** close to]{.underline} just [this kind of
**catastrophe**]{.underline} before.

On October 27, 1962 --- near the peak of the **[Cuban Missile
Crisis]{.underline}** --- an American [**U-2** reconnaissance
plane]{.underline} set out on a routine mission to the Arctic to collect
data on Soviet nuclear tests. But, [while **flying** near the North
Pole]{.underline}, with the stars obscured by the northern lights, the
[pilot made a **navigation error** and **strayed** into **Soviet
airspace**]{.underline}.1

[Soviet]{.underline} commanders [sent fighter jets to
**intercept**]{.underline} the American plane. The jets were [picked up
by **American radar** operators and **nuclear-armed F-102 fighters**
took off]{.underline} to protect the U-2.

Fortunately, the reconnaissance pilot realised his error with enough
time to correct course before the Soviet and American fighters met. But
the [**intrusion** **enraged** Soviet Premier **Nikita
Khrushchev**]{.underline}, who was [already on **high alert**
amidst]{.underline} the crisis in **[Cuba]{.underline}**.

"What is this, a provocation?" Khrushchev wrote to US President John F.
Kennedy. "One of your planes violates our frontier during this anxious
time when everything has been put into combat readiness."

[If the U-2's path had strayed **further west**]{.underline}, or the
Soviet fighters had been fast enough to intercept it, this
**[incident]{.underline}** could have [played out **quite differently**.
Both the United States and]{.underline} the [USSR had **thousands** of
**nuclear missiles** **ready to fire**]{.underline}. Instead of a
nearly-forgotten anecdote, the U-2 incident could have been a trigger
for war, like the assassination of Franz Ferdinand.

[**[Competition]{.mark}** among]{.underline} the [world's **most
powerful** countries]{.underline} shapes our world today. And whether
it's through [**future incidents** like the lost U-2]{.underline}, or
something else entirely, it's [**plausible** that it [could **escalate**
and lead to a
**major**]{.mark}, devastating **[war]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

Is there anything you can do to help avoid such a terrible outcome? It
is, of course, difficult to imagine how any one individual can hope to
influence such world-historical events. Even the most powerful world
leaders often fail to predict the global consequences of their
decisions.

But I think the **[likelihood]{.underline}** and
**[severity]{.underline}** of [great power **war** [makes this]{.mark}
among [the **most pressing** **problems**]{.mark} of our
**time**]{.underline} --- and that some solutions could be impactful
enough that working on them may be one of the highest-impact things to
do with your career.

By taking action, I think we can create a future where the threat of
great power war is a distant memory rather than an ever-present danger.

Summary

[Economic growth and
technological progress]{.underline} have [bolstered]{.underline} the
[arsenals]{.underline} of the world's most powerful countries. That
means the [**next war** between them could be **far
worse**]{.underline} than World War II, the deadliest conflict humanity
has yet experienced.

Could such a war actually occur? We can't rule out the
possibility. Technical accidents or [**diplomatic misunderstandings**
could **spark** a **conflict** that quickly escalates.
Or [**international tension** could cause **leaders** to decide
they're **better off** fighting than
**negotiating**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

It seems hard to make progress on this problem. It's also less neglected
than some of the problems that we think are most pressing. There are
certain issues, like making nuclear weapons or military artificial
intelligence systems safer, which seem promising --- although it may be
more impactful to work on reducing risks from AI, bioweapons or nuclear
weapons directly. You might also be able to reduce the chances of
misunderstandings and miscalculations by developing expertise in one of
the most important bilateral relationships (such as that between the
United States and China).

Finally, by making conflict less likely, reducing competitive pressures
on the development of dangerous technology, and improving international
cooperation, you might be helping to reduce other risks, like the chance
of future pandemics.

Our overall view

Working on this issue seems to be among the best ways of improving the
long-term future we know of, but all else equal, we think it's less
pressing than our highest priority areas (primarily because it seems
less neglected and harder to solve).

Scale

There's a **[significant chance]{.underline}** that a [**new** great
power war **occurs** this **century**]{.underline}.

Although the [world's **most powerful** countries]{.underline} haven't
fought directly since World War II, war has been
a [**constant** throughout **human history**]{.underline}. There have
been [**numerous** close
calls]{.underline}, and **[several issues]{.underline}** could
[cause **diplomatic disputes**]{.underline} in the years to come.

These considerations, along with forecasts and statistical models, lead
me to think there's about a one-in-three chance that a new great power
war breaks out in roughly the next 30 years.

Few wars cause more than a million casualties and the next great power
war would probably be smaller than that. However, there's some
[chance [it could **escalate massively**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Today the
[great powers have **much** [**larger economies**, **more powerful**
weapons, and **bigger**]{.mark} military **[budgets]{.mark}** than they
did in the past]{.underline}. An all-out war could kill far more
people than even World War II, the worst war we've yet experienced.

### Impact\-\--Imperialism\-\--2NC

#### Secession enables US encroachment\-\--Greenlandic independence creates a geopolitical vacuum that triggers U.S. interventionism to preempt rival influence, deepening militarization and neocolonial control.

**Thorsson '25** \[Elías; February 14; Editor and reporter for Arctic
Today; Arctic Today, "Senate hearing: Greenlandic independence could
force U.S. intervention,"
arctictoday.com/senate-hearing-greenlandic-independence-could-force-u-s-intervention\]
//jwh

"[Greenland's independence]{.underline}, if it happens, [will leave a
geopolitical **vacuum**]{.underline}. **[If the U.S. doesn't step in,
someone else will]{.underline}**," [said]{.underline} Alexander B. Gray,
former [**Deputy Assistant** to]{.underline} President
**[Trump]{.underline}** and Senior Fellow in National Security Affairs
at the American Foreign Policy Council, during his testimony.

The hearing, chaired by Sen. Ted Cruz (R-Texas), explored
[the]{.underline} economic and military [benefits of acquiring
Greenland]{.underline}, particularly [its **rich cache** of
rare]{.underline} earth **[minerals]{.underline}**, which he claims are
vital for U.S. industries.

"[**Acquiring** Greenland would have **enormous** economic
**benefits**]{.underline} for the United States," Cruz said.
"[The]{.underline} island's natural resources like [rare earth elements
would **strengthen** American supply chains]{.underline} and industries.
The island's strategic location in the Arctic would [provide **huge
advantages** in monitoring]{.underline} growing [**Russian** and
**Chinese bellicosity**]{.underline} in the region."

U.S. interests

[Greenland]{.underline}, which is currently an autonomous territory of
Denmark, [has been taking steps toward **full sovereignty**, a move
that]{.underline} could [leave it **politically**]{.underline} and
economically **[vulnerable]{.underline}**.

[If Greenland were to **break away** from Denmark without strong
**Western backing**]{.underline}, it could [become an **easy target**
for Chinese and Russian influence]{.underline}, Gray warned.

"[We need to be **proactive**]{.underline}, [not
**reactive**]{.underline}. If Greenland becomes independent, [we must be
the **first to offer** a deal]{.underline} that keeps them aligned with
the United States," he urged.

Two Options for U.S. Engagement

During his testimony, [**Gray** laid out]{.underline} two different
responses to Greenlandic independence:

[Turning Greenland into a **U.S. Territory**]{.underline}

This would place Greenland in a similar status as Puerto Rico or Guam,
[making it **part** of the U.S.]{.underline} while still allowing some
degree of self-governance.

The U.S. would [take control of **security**, **trade**, and **economic
development**]{.underline}, securing Greenland's vast natural resources
while expanding military operations in the Arctic.

#### That creates imperial conquest\-\--Threats of annexation provoke transatlantic backlash, collapse U.S. alliances, and trigger geopolitical isolation.

#### Greenland's dependence on Danish subsidies, weak economy, and geopolitical entanglements prevent sudden independence\-\--Long-term autonomy requires gradual integration, not rupture.

**EIIR '25** \[The European Institute for International Relations; March
24; Independent policy institute, based in Stockholm, staffed by
international lawyers and former US/EU officials, advises governments on
international relations and conflict resolution, conducts strategic
research on geopolitics and international law, launched the World Forum
on Peace and Security, primary source for diplomats and policymakers,
emphasizes objectivity and forward-looking legal analysis; The European
Institute for International Relations, "Greenland's Path to Sovereignty:
A Pragmatic Approach to Independence,"
eiir.eu/international-law/international-law-cases/greenlands-path-to-sovereignty-a-pragmatic-approach-to-independence\]
//jwh

[Greenland remains **very** dependent on Denmark]{.underline} with
annual subsidies amounting to nearly one-third of its GDP. The biggest
challenge towards becoming a sovereign Nation is reducing its economic
dependence while ensuring that potential international partnerships and
alliances do not result in undue foreign influence. In order to achieve
this, [Greenland]{.underline} must find viable sectors, such as
renewable resources, fisheries, and responsible mining, to create a
sovereign economy. Additionally, it [must also **shield** itself
against]{.underline} excessive [dependence on foreign
powers]{.underline}, most [notably]{.underline} China and [the
**U.S**]{.underline}., [which are]{.underline} becoming [**very**
interested in the rare earth minerals]{.underline} within Greenland.
Meanwhile, the [path towards]{.underline} complete [sovereignty
**must**]{.underline} also [occur in a **gradual** manner]{.underline}.
It should be a step-by-step process by which the nation would
increasingly assume control of core areas such as foreign affairs and
the management of natural resources while maintaining economic and
political linkages with the Danish government. This would prevent the
nation from facing abrupt economic instability or pressures from the
global world which would counteract its long-term objectives.

Greenland's path towards autonomy is not occurring in isolation. The
geopolitical value of the island means the global powers including the
U.S. and NATO are also invested in ensuring stability within the region.
The Arctic is becoming the new arena for geopolitical competition with
increased military presence from Russia and China. As Greenland heads
toward autonomy, it needs to proactively interact with the institutions
of the region such as the Arctic Council to make itself a cooperative
and responsible partner. Furthermore, good diplomatic relations with the
U.S. and Denmark are also crucial. The fact that there is Thule Air Base
is a testament to Washington's strategic interest and therefore
Greenland must treat its relationship with the U.S. with caution. Any
future agreements regarding the military presence must be open and
beneficial for the future security and economic advancement of
Greenland.

Moreover, sovereignty for Greenland [must]{.underline} also
**[prioritize]{.underline}** the [**well-being** of]{.underline} its
[aboriginal people]{.underline}. It is imperative that [its
path]{.underline} to independence [does **not** come at the **expense**
of social cohesion]{.underline} or environmental stewardship. The
policies must be written with the preservation of cultural identity, the
establishment of economic opportunities for all citizens, and the
protection against environmental degradation associated with resource
extraction.

#### Greenland lacks the capacity to manage defense or governance alone\-\--Leaders admit independence is utopian in the short-term and risks U.S. domination without Denmark's backing.

**Milne '25** \[Richard; Nordic and Baltic Bureau Chief for The
Financial Times, former capital markets editor, European business
correspondent, Frankfurt and Paris correspondents, won the business and
finance journalist of the year in 2015 at the Press Awards; The
Financial Times, "Greenland independence debate 'on steroids' ahead of
election," ft.com/content/4c657512-56a8-411b-819b-b8e78f07767f\] //jwh

"There's a [**misconception** that Greenland is going to be independent
**tomorrow**]{.underline}," said Chemnitz, MP for IA. "[It is a
**long-term** goal]{.underline}."

Relations with Denmark have long been complicated by scandals over
enforced sterilisation of Greenlanders and the racism they have faced.
Egede said in his new year address that Greenland needed to throw off
"the shackles of colonialism".

Some argue Greenland has come a long way. It received home rule in 1979
and more rights 30 years later, including to seek independence. "If we
should define ourselves, we should say we are the only nation of
indigenous people who have their own right to their land and resources,"
said Aqqaluk Lynge, founder of IA. "You don't find that arrangement
anywhere else in the world."

But Greenland has not used all its potential powers. More than 30 still
reside with Denmark, including policing, defence and security. Some in
the capital Nuuk argue [it will take **decades** to be able to
**afford** to run]{.underline} most [things independently]{.underline}.

But not everyone is prepared to wait that long. Naleraq, the party to
which the two Siumut politicians defected, has welcomed Trump's interest
and said they want to start independence talks with Copenhagen straight
away.

Kuno Fencker, a Greenlandic MP who is one of the defectors, said: "It's
good for Greenland to get all this attention. Suddenly Denmark is
behaving very nicely. Suddenly they could solve some problems
immediately."

Even he, however, sees the limits of Greenland's push for nationhood.
"[We know we can't be **fully** independent]{.underline} . . . [It's
**utopia** to think that Greenland can handle]{.underline} defence and
security with [a population of **57,000**]{.underline} people and an
area larger than western Europe . . . The US will be the ones to defend
this island," he added, pointing at the current US base, the main
military installation on Greenland, on the north of the island.

Denmark itself has reacted cautiously, with Prime Minister Mette
Frederiksen keen not to inflame tensions with either the US or
Greenland. She has said Greenlanders' desire for independence is
"legitimate and understandable". Many in Nuuk say she is the most
friendly Danish prime minister ever towards them.

Some Danish politicians argue [Greenland]{.underline} would be [**best**
sticking with Copenhagen]{.underline}, otherwise they risk being snapped
up by the US. "This [US **aggression** makes it **harder** for Greenland
to become independent]{.underline}. Then they would have to face the
Americans alone, rather than together with Denmark," said Rasmus Jarlov,
a former minister and current Conservative MP.

#### Three-quarters of Greenlanders voted for cautious autonomy over full independence\-\--Election results show overwhelming rejection of rushed separation from Denmark.

**Filippov '25** \[Anton; March 13; Journalist at European Pravda;
European Pravda, "How Greenland chose between independence, Denmark, and
the US and what's next,"
eurointegration.com.ua/eng/news/2025/03/13/7207063\] //jwh

[Greenland]{.underline}, once a colony and now an autonomous territory
of the Kingdom of Denmark, gained the right to have its own parliament
in 1979.

The island [does **not** make independent decisions]{.underline} in
matters [related to the]{.underline} national
**[constitution]{.underline}**, **[citizenship]{.underline}**, [the
**S**]{.underline}upreme **[C]{.underline}**ourt,
**[foreign]{.underline}**, **[defense]{.underline}**, [and **security
policy**]{.underline}, as well as monetary policy.

In return, Greenland receives an annual subsidy from Denmark of over
€500 million for social welfare. Given the island\'s population of fewer
than 57,000, this translates to more than €17,000 per capita.

During this campaign, Danish officials made a point of emphasising their
non-interference in Greenlandic affairs, reaffirming in response to
Trump that \"the island is not for sale\" and that its fate lies solely
in the hands of its residents.

Pre-election polls showed that while most Greenlanders favour
[independence]{.underline}, there is [**no consensus** on **when** it
should happen or **how** it would **impact**]{.underline} their standard
of [living]{.underline}.

As a result, three-quarters of [Greenlanders]{.underline} voted for
parties advocating a cautious approach to independence.

Unexpectedly, the center-right Democrats party emerged as the winner,
securing nearly 30% of the vote (compared to just 9% in the previous
election).

Historically [**sceptical** of **full** independence]{.underline}, the
Democrats favour expanded autonomy within the Danish Kingdom. Although
their platform envisions an independent Greenland with a free-market
economy, they [warn against **hasty moves** that]{.underline} could
[**harm** people's livelihoods]{.underline} and the economy.

#### Full independence would sever Danish aid and impose massive sovereign burdens\-\--No feasible path exists to cover defense, welfare, and institutional costs solo.

**Mortensen '25** \[Henrik; January 10; Vice President at Liberal
International, chairman of the Danish Institute of Parties and
Democracy, Board member of the Danish-Taiwanese Society, vice President
in ALDE, chairman of the ALDE policy committee on EU-China relations,
member of the RENEW Task Force for elections to the European Parliament
in 2024; Liberal International, "Endgame in Greenland,"
liberal-international.org/news-articles/endgame-in-greenland\] //jwh

[If Greenland achieves **full** independence]{.underline}, it's likely
that all economic transfers from Denmark would cease. Additionally, the
Danish Parliament and government need to decide whether future citizens
of Greenland should have visa-free access to Denmark and the right to
residence permits in the country. Questions also arise about whether
there should be access to the Danish job market and if specialized
services in health care and higher education should remain available.

[How Greenland will **manage**]{.underline} its [foreign and security
policy remains **uncertain**]{.underline}, although the 1951 Defense
Agreement outlines U.S. rights concerning Greenland. If Greenland aims
to pursue its own interests regarding foreign, defense, and security
policies, Greenlanders must clarify how this will be managed. This would
[involve **insurmountable** economic **costs**]{.underline} if Greenland
were responsible for bearing the expenses of enforcing sovereignty,
maintaining a standing defense, and other military capabilities. Hence,
[full]{.underline} independence with all its [implications
is]{.underline} nearly [an **impossibility**]{.underline} for Greenland.

Another possibility might involve negotiating a new status for
Greenland's relationship with Denmark in connection with independence.
Greenland could gain independence while the [Danish
government]{.underline} and Parliament agree to [**continue**
funding]{.underline} substantial financial support for Greenland.
Additionally, individual rights of Greenlanders within Danish society
might be maintained. However, it seems [**unrealistic** that Denmark
would **bear** these costs]{.underline} or grant such privileges if
foreign and security policy matters were disentangled from the Kingdom
of Denmark and [made **sovereignly** controlled by
Greenland]{.underline}. Furthermore, there remains uncertainty regarding
how an independent Greenland's defense and security policy situation
would be handled.

#### The election outcome signals dual rejection of U.S. acquisition and rapid independence.

GDP virtually overnight would be catastrophic. Furthermore, the
Greenlandic economy serves a relatively small population spread sparsely
across the country. Current economic conditions are simply not conducive
to Greenland becoming independent (Mazza, 2015).

## Alaska CP

### Alaska CP\-\--1NC

#### The United States Federal Government should increase its development and exploration of minerals in ALASKA.

#### **We got Greenland at home\-\-- its called Alaska!!**

**Milbank '19** \[Dana; August 22; Columnist with *The Washington Post*;
*Washington Post*, "We already have Greenland. It's called Alaska."
https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2019/08/22/we-already-have-greenland-its-called-alaska/\]

This week, the American people and [the [world have become much more
familiar with **Greenland**]{.mark},]{.underline} the world's largest
island. We know that two U.S. presidents, Harry Truman (in 1946) and
[Donald Trump (Friday) have expressed interest in purchasing
it.]{.underline} President Truman **quoted a price of **\$100 million in
1946; we never learned the figure President Trump had in mind. [But the
offer was moot before it was proffered[**: Greenland is not for sale**,
**but** very much "]{.mark}]{.underline}**[open for business]{.mark}**,"
according to Greenland's foreign minister and the prime minister of
Denmark.

Over the past few days, there have been many questions about the
possible motivations behind the president's interest in and enthusiasm
for Greenland. The next bold real estate move[? A strategic gambit to
bolster U.S. military presence in the Arctic? Or a wily decision to
further cement America's economic dominance?]{.underline} On the latter,
the economics of the Arctic are important to understand.

Six years ago, my think tank conducted a study in which [we examined the
economic benefits of a **[rapidly transforming]{.underline}**
Arctic]{.mark}. While we focused on the United States, our report did
examine Greenland and its rich mineral resources, which include rare
earth elements, uranium, iron ore, gold, diamonds, lead and zinc.
Greenland, like [[Alaska]{.underline}]{.mark}, Canada and Russia[[, is
home to some of the world's largest nickel, iron ore and zinc
mines.]{.underline}]{.mark}

The discovery of[ ]{.underline}large reserves of rare earth metals is
what has probably [piqued Chinese economic interest in
Greenland]{.underline}. Rare earth elements are essential to a range of
smartphones and other devices, but they also have critical applications
in the defense, automotive and renewal energy industries, including in
wind turbines, hybrid-vehicle motors, missile-guidance systems,
rechargeable batteries for consumer products, night-vision goggles,
alloys for sonar technology and lasers in weapons systems.
The Ilimaussaq complex on the southwestern coast of Greenland holds the
largest quantities of rare earth elements in the world, with an
inventory of 10.3 million tons of rare earth oxide. This same complex
also holds 575 million pounds of uranium concentrates, as well as 2.21
million tons of zinc, which would meet a great deal of global demand for
the next 50 years. [Several Chinese companies are already actively
mining in Greenland.]{.underline} The United States has not made any
comparable investments in the island.

In 2008, the U.S. Geological Survey estimated that the [[Arctic could
hold upwards of 30 percent of the world's undiscovered gas]{.mark}
resources and 13 percent of the world undiscovered oil
resources,]{.underline} with an estimated 84 percent located in offshore
areas. While the survey again brought a greater focus on Alaska's
extensive resources, believed to hold the second largest Arctic energy
reserves in the world, Greenland also drew attention. So far, however,
the **[[envisioned Greenland oil boom has not panned
out]{.underline}]{.mark}**. In 2013, a British firm abandoned its
exploration efforts off the coast of Greenland after its \$1.4 billion
venture did not yield predicted reserves.

And in a world with a rapidly diminishing Arctic polar ice cap,
increased Arctic shipping --- both across the Arctic and through the
Central Passage or trans-Polar route --- could economically benefit
Greenland. Investment in port infrastructure and related maritime
activity would make Greenland a potential shipping hub for trans-Polar
commerce. China is exploring the possibilities of this trans-polar sea
route as the Chinese shipping firm COSCO annually tests this route. But
[[Greenland's]{.mark} small population (]{.underline}57,000[) and
[limited infrastructure]{.mark} (predominantly along the southwestern
coast) [make it less attractive]{.mark}]{.underline}. Iceland seems to
be a more attractive location for enhanced port infrastructure (which a
German firm is pursuing) for future Chinese exports.

One of the least discussed reasons that China is interested in exploring
economic opportunities in the Arctic is the country's continued access
to the rich and growing fishing stocks in the Arctic. In fact, access to
fishing stocks is so economically vital to Arctic nations that they have
raised tensions and led to the potential for conflict. One example is
the so-called "cod wars\" between Iceland and Britain in the 1970s.

With all the discussion about the economic importance of Greenland, the
ironic part of this tale is that [Arctic mineral and energy
resources,]{.underline} [key shipping infrastructure and fisheries
already exist in the United States]{.underline} today \... [in
Alaska,]{.underline} a strategic purchase the United States made from
the Russian empire in 1867 for a mere \$7.2 million. [Wouldn't it be
better to wisely and sustainably invest in the American
Arctic?]{.underline} The American Arctic urgently needs a deepwater port
to responsibly manage an increase in shipping traffic through the narrow
Bering Strait. Although it is controversial and must be balanced with
strong environmental protection[, [the United States can **enhance** its
mineral and energy extraction from Alaska]{.mark} while working toward
diversifying its resource-based economy[. **Rather than buy Greenland**,
the **US should invest in Alaska** and deepen our economic and security
engagement with Greenland and Denmark.]{.mark} After all, both are open
for business.]{.underline}

https://www.wsj.com/articles/alaskan-mining-is-superior-to-greenland-mining-daniel-sullivan-trump-biden-ambler-railroad-arctic-defense-8f2a4b1a\]

[[There's been a lot of talk about President-elect Trump's idea of

while promoting environmental stewardship and community engagement
