### AT: Prolif\-\--1NC

#### No prolif impact\-\--[too many barriers]{.underline} and [no incentive]{.underline}.

Nicholas **Miller 22**, associate professor of government at Dartmouth
College, author of the book *Stopping the Bomb: The Sources and
Effectiveness of U.S. Nonproliferation Policy*, 10/17/2022, \"Why the
War in Ukraine Won't Spark a Nuclear Proliferation Cascade,\"
https://foreignpolicy.com/2022/10/17/ukraine-war-russia-nuclear-proliferation-iran-south-korea/

Soon after Russian President Vladimir Putin launched his renewed
invasion of Ukraine in February, analysts began warning of dire
consequences for the global nuclear order.

Russia's invasion, the argument goes, shows that nuclear weapons can
provide a shield that enables conquest. After all, Putin has used
nuclear threats from the outset of the war to help deter Western
intervention, and U.S. President Joe Biden himself has made clear the
prospect of a nuclear confrontation with Russia, powerfully shaping his
caution about direct U.S. involvement.

At the same time, [Russian aggression shows how difficult it is for
**nonnuclear** states to deter]{.underline} their nuclear [adversaries.
According to this]{.underline} line of [argument, if only Ukraine had
its own **nuclear arsenal**, Russia **wouldn't** have dared
attack]{.underline} it---just like the United States probably wouldn't
have attacked a nuclear-armed Iraq under former President Saddam Hussein
or a nuclear-armed Libya under former leader Muammar al-Qaddafi.
(Ukraine relinquished the Soviet nuclear weapons, which it never had
operational control over, that were left on its territory in the 1990s
after the Soviet Union's collapse.)

The lesson thus seems clear: Whether you're a status quo power or a
dissatisfied aggressor, you'll be better off with the bomb. According to
Yale University historian Timothy Snyder, who has taken the argument
perhaps the farthest, unless Ukraine dispels these lessons by defeating
Russia, the result will be "global [nuclear
**[prolif]{.mark}**eration]{.underline}."

[[Commentators]{.mark} are]{.underline} often [[**not specific**
about]{.underline}]{.mark} exactly [[which
countries]{.underline}]{.mark} they believe [will [take part]{.mark} in
this **cascade**]{.underline}, but [one can imagine **Iran** concluding
a nuclear arsenal]{.underline} would give it more leeway in the Middle
East, which would likely then lead Saudi Arabia to pursue a bomb
program. Likewise, [**Taiwan** or **So**uth **Ko**rea]{.underline} might
become increasingly worried about facing an invasion by their
nuclear-armed adversaries, causing them to seek a nuclear arsenal.

[Although]{.underline} the [logic of these [arg]{.mark}ument[s]{.mark}
is persuasive at **first glance**, they [are
**not**]{.mark}]{.underline} well [**[supported]{.mark}**
by]{.underline} the **[history]{.underline}** of nuclear proliferation,
in large part because [they [overlook]{.mark}]{.underline} the
**[[complexity of nuclear decision-making]{.underline}]{.mark}**.

[From]{.underline} the **[1950s]{.underline}** onward,
[[analysts]{.underline}]{.mark} and government officials [have been
[predicting **prolif**]{.mark}eration cascades or **domino
effects**]{.underline}, where there would be a rapid increase in the
number of states with nuclear weapons. Most famously, then-U.S.
President John F. Kennedy warned in 1963 that he foresaw the possibility
of as many as 25 nuclear-armed states by 1980 unless the international
community got a handle on the problem.

[These predictions]{.underline} were **[[never borne
out]{.underline}]{.mark}**: The [number of states]{.underline} with
nuclear weapons [has grown rather **slowly**]{.underline}, especially
since the 1970s. Between 1945 and 1970, six states acquired the bomb
(about one every four years). Since then, only four countries have
additionally done so (about one every 13 years).

Why has [[prolif]{.mark}eration [proceeded]{.mark} so
**[slowly]{.mark}**]{.underline}? In large part [because
leaders]{.underline} like Kennedy [worried about it [and]{.mark}
adapted]{.underline} their [policies to **prevent** proliferation
cascades]{.underline}. Especially since the 1960s, a [[**powerful**
architecture]{.mark} for [prevent]{.mark}ing]{.underline} the
[[spread]{.underline}]{.mark} of nuclear weapons [has been
constructed]{.underline}. This includes [the
**[N]{.mark}**on-**[P]{.mark}**roliferation **[T]{.mark}**reaty,
economic **[sanctions]{.mark}**]{.underline} policies adopted by the
United States and others, [the **[N]{.mark}**uclear
**[S]{.mark}**uppliers **[G]{.mark}**roup, and]{.underline} sometimes
[the [threat]{.mark} and use [of force]{.mark}]{.underline}---all of
which [have]{.underline} been shown to have **[restrained]{.underline}**
the [spread of nuclear weapons.]{.underline}

This [[architecture is]{.underline}]{.mark} still [in place **today**
and]{.underline} in some ways [[**stronger** than]{.mark} it has
[ever]{.mark} been]{.underline}. Today, almost every state without
nuclear weapons is a member of the Non-Proliferation Treaty, and perhaps
for the first time since 1945, [there are **[no
states]{.mark}**]{.underline} currently known to be [[seeking
nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}]{.underline}. (**[Iran]{.underline}**
is producing enriched uranium very close to weapons grade, but according
to the U.S. intelligence community, as of December 2021, there is [**no
evidence** it has made]{.underline} the [decision to build a
bomb]{.underline}.)

We also know from recent research that proliferation outcomes are not
determined by security incentives in the mechanistic way predicted by
those worried about the war in Ukraine's impact. In other words,
[states]{.underline} that [you may **think**]{.underline} would
[**want** nuclear weapons]{.underline} for protection or aggression
[often do **not**]{.underline} try to get them, [and [states]{.mark}
that try to get them]{.underline} sometimes [[**fail** due to
**domestic**]{.mark} **pathologies** or
**[divisions]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

Many [states in threatening]{.underline} security [environments are
satisfied with]{.underline} relying on a [nuclear-armed
**ally**]{.underline} rather than seeking their own arsenals, despite
perennial concerns over the credibility of extended deterrence.
[[Japan]{.mark} is]{.underline} a **[classic example]{.underline}**: It
has [considered developing nuclear weapons]{.underline} at various
points since the 1950s [but]{.underline} has
[[concluded]{.underline}]{.mark} it's [[better off relying
on]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates]{.underline} than taking the risk of producing an
arsenal of its own.

Other states, such as [**Sweden** and **[Germany]{.mark}**]{.underline},
over time [[bought into]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[**norm against**
developing nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s]{.mark}]{.underline}. In some cases,
such as [**Argentina** and **Brazil**]{.underline} in the 1980s, there
[are]{.underline} domestic [**divisions** over]{.underline} the [utility
of nuclear weapons]{.underline}, and factions end up splitting the
difference by developing civilian nuclear technology without developing
an arsenal right away. [For]{.underline} countries highly dependent on
the international economy, such as [**Taiwan** or the **U**nited
**A**rab **E**mirates]{.underline}, the potential [**costs** of going
nuclear]{.underline} may **[outweigh]{.underline}** the security
[benefits]{.underline}.

[Even [countries]{.mark} that decide they **want** nuclear
weapons]{.underline} to protect themselves against (or coerce) an
adversary [often **[fail to acquire them]{.mark}**. **Authoritarian
states**]{.underline} in particular tend to [**mismanage** their
**nuclear programs**]{.underline}, as the cases of Iraq and Libya
illustrate.

All this evidence aside, one could still argue there is something unique
about the current conflict in **[[Ukraine]{.underline}]{.mark}** that
would overwhelm these historical dynamics. But this [[is **far**
from]{.mark} the **[first time]{.mark}** a [nuclear]{.mark}-armed
[state]{.mark} has [attacked]{.mark} a [nonnuclear]{.mark}
state]{.underline}, nor is it the first time a nuclear state has used
its nuclear arsenal to backstop coercion or aggression.

In [**1967**, Israel]{.underline} launched an [attack]{.underline} that
[resulted in]{.underline} the [occupation of]{.underline} the Golan
Heights, **[Gaza Strip]{.underline}**, West Bank, and Sinai Peninsula.
[Israel]{.underline} reportedly [considered unveiling]{.underline} its
recently acquired [nuclear arsenal if necessary to stave off defeat.
During]{.underline} the **[Vietnam War]{.underline}**, then-U.S.
President Richard [Nixon attempted to use]{.underline} veiled [nuclear
threats to coerce]{.underline} the [Soviets]{.underline} into pressuring
the North Vietnamese on peace negotiations. [Pakistan]{.underline} has
repeatedly [used]{.underline} its [**nuclear arsenal** as]{.underline} a
[cover for aggressive actions against India. Russia annexed Crimea
and]{.underline} occupied [parts of]{.underline} eastern
[Ukraine]{.underline} in 2014 while [knowing it could use its nuclear
arsenal]{.underline} if necessary [to deter]{.underline} outside
[intervention]{.underline}.

[[In **no**]{.mark}**ne** of these [cases]{.mark}]{.underline} of
nuclear-backed aggression [[did]{.mark} **anything** approaching a
cascade of [prolif]{.mark}eration **[result]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Nor
did the regime-change operations against Iraq and Libya---countries that
had given up their nuclear weapons programs under pressure---lead to a
rush for the bomb (though they may have helped convince North Korea to
hold on to what it has).

Is it possible that some countries might rethink their security options
as a result of what happens in Ukraine? Absolutely. But
[[countries]{.mark} are]{.underline} just as (or perhaps
**[more]{.underline}**) [likely to conclude]{.underline} that [they are
[better off finding]{.mark}]{.underline} or getting closer to [a
**[nuclear]{.mark}-armed [ally]{.mark}**]{.underline}---another
important form of protection Ukraine lacked, being outside of NATO---as
they are seeking their own arsenals. Even if one or two
**[countries]{.underline}** decide to seek a bomb, they [will have to
overcome **substantial obstacles** to **successfully acquire
one**]{.underline}.

There are good strategic reasons to hope Ukraine prevails---for
instance, it could help reinforce the norm against territorial conquest
and possibly reduce the odds of future Russian aggression---but the case
should not rest on averting [a **[cascade of
prolif]{.mark}eration**]{.underline} that [[is **unlikely**]{.mark} to
**materialize** in any event]{.underline}.

## Solvency

### Greenland Says No\-\--1NC

#### Greenland has no interest in mining at a large scale

**Bradstock '25** \[Felicity; June 29; Master's in International
Development, freelance Energy and Finance writer; OilPrice.com; "Why
Greenland Isn't Chasing the Dream of Becoming a Mining Superpower";
https://oilprice.com/Metals/Commodities/Why-Greenland-Isnt-Chasing-the-Dream-of-Becoming-a-Mining-Superpower.html\] 

[Greenland]{.mark}, despite possessing vast mineral reserves and past
ambitions from the U.S. to acquire it for these resources, [is **[not
interested in becoming a major mining superpower]{.underline}**]{.mark}.

[The country\'s minister]{.mark} for business and mineral resources
[[emphasized a desire for **only 5 to 10 active**]{.underline}]{.mark}
[**[mines at any given time]{.mark},** [prioritizing high
environmental]{.mark}]{.underline}, social, and governance
[[standards]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Concerns about the environmental impact and the uncertain economic
return from mining activities, particularly with an unstable market for
rare earths, contribute to Greenland\'s cautious approach.

Greenland

Early in Donald Trump's presidency, he announced his ambitions to
acquire Greenland. In March, Trump said, "We need Greenland for
international safety and security. We need it. We have to have it." As
well as stating ambitions to counter Russia's presence in the Arctic,
taking control of Greenland would put vast quantities of rare earth
minerals and rare earth elements in the possession of the United States.
However, Denmark quickly shot down the idea of Trump's acquisition,
saying that Greenland was not for sale. Meanwhile, Trump's announcement
led several political figures in Greenland to suggest that Greenland
should be independent, rather than under the ownership of Denmark or the
U.S. 

International powers have long eyed Greenland for its vast mineral
potential. As several countries around the world strive to undergo a
green transition, the demand for critical minerals is expected to grow
dramatically in the coming years. Demand for critical energy transition
minerals like lithium, cobalt, and copper could increase almost fourfold
by 2030, according to United Nations estimates. 

At present, China dominates the global mineral mining market. According
to International Energy Agency (IEA) data, China contributes around 80
percent of the world's natural graphite and 60 percent of mined magnet
rare earths. In 2024, it produced more than 60 percent of the world's
lithium, 40 percent of refined copper, and 70 percent of refined cobalt.
As the U.S. looks to reduce its reliance on China for energy, critical
minerals, and other goods, President Trump sees Greenland as the
potential solution. 

Greenland holds vast mineral reserves, including rare earth metals,
coal, graphite, uranium, copper, lead, and zinc. A 2023 Geological
Survey of Denmark and Greenland survey found that 25 of the 34 critical
raw materials recognised by the European Commission are present in
Greenland. In addition, [as ice]{.mark} sheets [melt]{.mark} due to
climate change, [[Greenland's previously hard-to-retrieve mineral
reserves are expected to become more accessible]{.underline}]{.mark}. 

Caroline Kennedy-Pipe, a professor of war studies and Arctic security
specialist at the U.K.'s Loughborough University, explained, "The fight
for infrastructure in the Arctic is incredibly important. Plus, because
Greenland's ice sheet is melting, and melting fast, those rare earth
minerals will become cheaper to access. So, these are seen as long-term
investments for America."

To date, mining activity [in Greenland]{.mark} has been limited, as
[[investors have avoided financing mining]{.mark} [operations due to the
**harsh conditions** and **environmental pushback**]{.mark}]{.underline}
from community groups. [[Developing]{.mark} [the country's mining
industry would **require significant funding**]{.mark}]{.underline}, as
well as support from local groups. 

Around a decade ago, a team of geologists published a paper that warned
of unrealistic expectations for Greenland's mineral potential. It
states, "[[Even if estimates of the quantity and quality of ore in a
geological deposit are well documented... it is **difficult to translate
this into economic**]{.underline}]{.mark} potential and even more
difficult to predict a specific **[[revenue]{.underline}]{.mark}** for
Greenlandic society." 

Minik Thorleif Rosing, one of the authors of the report, said that the
situation remains relatively unchanged since the date of publication.
"There is a misconception that Greenland will be like a new Saudi
Arabia, only at the size of a small British town," Rosing said. He
blamed the uncertainty on global market conditions for rare earth
metals. [There has been significant pushback against mining
operations,]{.underline} as many Greenlanders believe **[[the cost of
mining will far outweigh the benefit]{.underline}]{.mark}**. It is
unclear just how much of a return on investment Greenland would see from
mining activities, as [[there is no stable market for rare]{.mark}
[earths]{.mark}]{.underline}, according to Rosing. However, many are
more concerned about the environmental impact of mining in the Arctic. 

In June, Naaja Nathanielsen, [[Greenland's minister]{.underline}]{.mark}
for business and mineral resources, said that while some mining
operations are already underway in the country, there is little interest
in Greenland becoming a major mining power. "For Greenland, we are not
necessarily interested in becoming a really great mining country. We
just really want 5 or 10 active mines at any given time," said
Nathanielsen. "We are a very small population, so for us, we don't need
the entire country to be covered in mines. We are happy with managing a
few, and I think that is feasible," she added. 

Nathanielsen [[emphasised the "**very high"
environmental**]{.underline},]{.mark} social and governance (ESG)
[[**standards** held by]{.mark} [Greenland]{.mark}]{.underline}. She
said, "I think the people of Greenland really support the mining
industry, which is quite kind of rare when you look at other
jurisdictions. But they do so because they have faith in us having a
high environmental standard and taking care of local communities."
Nathanielsen went on to say that [[if the]{.mark} [government

lead [[Greenlanders]{.underline}]{.mark} [to [no longer support]{.mark}
[the mining industry]{.mark}]{.underline}, which could be highly

### L\-\--Security\-\--1NC

#### U.S. securitization of Greenland mirrors the War on Terror's logic\-\--Faming strategic landscapes as existential threats justifies imperial extraction, entrenches global militarism, and unleashes cascading securitization across regions and sectors.

**Gad '24** \[Ulrik, Marc Jacobsen, and Ole Wæver, and Ulrik Pram Gad;
February 14; Senior Researcher for Global security and worldview at the
Danish Institute for International Studies; Danish Institute for
International Studies, "Greenland\'s desecuritization of security and
defense," 10.3998/mpub.12676130\] //jwh

In the summer of 2019, Donald [Trump's idea of]{.underline} buying
[Greenland drew **renewed attention** to its]{.underline} geostrategic
[location and revealed **widespread misunderstandings** of
its]{.underline} current [constitutional status]{.underline}. Trump
equated the idea to similar past purchase attempts, but since times of
colonization Greenland had taken several steps to increase its autonomy.
This meant that if a purchase should be proposed, it should have been
addressed to the Government of Greenland, which, prompted or not, did
not fail to respond on twitter: "We're open for business, not for sale"
(GreenlandMFA 2019). While many ridiculed the outdated idea,
[it]{.underline} soon [became apparent that it **reflected**
a]{.underline} more [profound [**shift** in the U.S. **security**
perspective on the Arctic region]{.mark}]{.underline} as being embedded
in international great power competition with Russia and, especially,
China. In response, [the United States [looked to Greenland with the
**aim** of **strengthening** its]{.mark} regional [**presence** and
securing access to]{.mark}]{.underline} strategically [important
**[minerals]{.mark}**]{.underline} such as rare earth elements that are
critical components in many types of modern and so-called 'critical'
technologies.

Whereas the dual awareness of natural resources and convenient military
strategic location [[reflected the **root** of the U.S. interest in
Greenland]{.underline}]{.mark}, the international power relations [and
the American security **perspectives**]{.underline} have changed
throughout history. The aim of this chapter is to provide the first
coherent analysis of the defining acts [[shaping]{.underline}]{.mark}
the [**development** of U.S. [security **interests**]{.mark} in
Greenland during the past **200 years**]{.underline}. In order to do so,
we dissect how [securitization acts]{.underline} and attempts have been
carried out at specific times in history by pointing to perceived
threats as legitimization of extraordinary means. When doing so, we
employ the Copenhagen School's analytical apparatus as explained in the
introductory chapter while paying special attention to the scales,
audiences, and cascading effects of the particular securitizations. This
means that we are extra attentive to whether the perceived threats have
been articulated as, for instance, an international, regional, or
national matter, whether the securitizations have [had effects
beyond]{.underline} the [described purposes]{.underline}, and [who [the
United States]{.mark}]{.underline} has [[considered]{.underline}]{.mark}
relevant [[recipients with agency to **accept** or **refuse** the
**securitization**]{.mark} act]{.underline}. We believe that these three
specific foci---which we will soon explain in more details---provide
effective tools in finding answers to our research questions, which are:
Why has United States securitized Greenland, how have securitizations
been received, and with what consequences?

When answering these questions, we show how [securitizations have been
[**discursively** constructed as]{.mark}]{.underline} part of
[overarching security **developments** in which [the
U.S.]{.mark}]{.underline} has [[considered Greenland a **geostrategic**
piece of land]{.underline}]{.mark} in the protection of U.S.
self-interests and its balance of power against shifting enemies. As we
shall see, the articulated [reasons of the securitization acts have
varied]{.underline} by sometimes pointing to referent objects at other
scales such as 'Western hemisphere,' 'NATO area,' and 'international
peace' in the quest to gain acceptance of extraordinary means. [[The
**choice** of words]{.underline}]{.mark} has to some extent been
[[guided by]{.mark} the **[congruent agency]{.mark}** ascribed
[to]{.mark}]{.underline} Denmark and [[Greenland]{.mark},
which]{.underline} at different times in history, and in different ways,
[have been [considered **inactive spectators**]{.mark}]{.underline} or
as part of the relevant audience with power to provide formal or moral
support. The [U.S. rhetoric]{.underline}, however, has occasionally
[been [a play to]{.mark} the gallery]{.underline}, as the extraordinary
means in a few instances have been carried out prior to the
**[[rhetorical securitization]{.underline}]{.mark}**, hence highlighting
the unequal power relationship and the lack of depth of actual
acknowledged agency. In other instances, Denmark has both acted as the
accepting audience and carried out the extraordinary means even though
it **[jeopardized]{.underline}** domestic laws or entailed negative
consequences at lower scales. It is important to note that throughout
most of the historical period analyzed in this chapter, Greenland was a
colony to Denmark, thereby being ruled out in the U.S.-Denmark decision
making and deliberations roughly until after home rule was introduced in
1979. By focusing on [**cascading** effects of U.S.
securitizations]{.underline}, we seek to bring attention to some of the
derived consequences that, regrettably, are [**seldom** part
of]{.underline} analyses of [U.S. security **interests** in
Greenland]{.underline} and the Arctic.

Our analysis is divided into the six analytical periods of 1823--1914,
1914--39, 1939--45, 1945--91, 1991--2018, and 2018--21, delimited by the
signing of the Monroe Doctrine, the outbreak of World War I, the
beginning and end of World War II, the ensuing Cold War, its end, and
the most recent, and still active, U.S. security perspective on
Greenland and the Arctic where China and Russia are seen as great power
competitors. This periodization rests upon a U.S. perspective of widely
acknowledged shifts in the American geopolitical visions that have
happened alongside U.S. securitization acts and attempts regarding
Greenland.1 Before turning to the analysis of these time spans, we will
now explain our choice of theoretical tools and our selection of
empirical data.

In continuation of the introduction's basic explanation of
[securitization theory]{.underline}, we will here [focus on scales,
**[cascading effects]{.mark}**, and audiences]{.underline} that we find
particularly relevant in the effort to get a better understanding of why
securitizing acts have been executed, how they have been received, and
with what consequences.

First, we are especially attentive to what scale [the referent
**object** is **discursively** placed on]{.underline} because it can
help us uncover what or who exactly the securitizing actor pointed to as
being threatened in the attempt to convince the relevant audience of
accepting the use of extraordinary means. Was it, for instance, [[a
**threat** to]{.underline}]{.mark} '[the **West**]{.underline},'
[[to]{.underline}]{.mark} '**[[regional Arctic
peace]{.underline}]{.mark}**,' or to 'U.S. sovereignty' that [was
[articulated as the reason for]{.mark} a **specific** U.S.
**[securitization]{.mark}**]{.underline} with relation to Greenland at
some point in history? By paying special attention to the prioritized
scale in a securitization (attempt), we seek to dissect the given reason
for legitimizing the use of extraordinary means and to draw attention to
the consequences the prioritization of one scale may have had for
entities at other scales given less or no attention in the
securitization act. When applying this approach, we rely on Buzan and
Wæver's (2009) article in which they lay out connections across the
spectrum of scales as spanning from the global to the individual with
system, civilizational, unit, and groups covering the middle ground
(2009, 259). Among these, [the **Copenhagen** School]{.underline} has
traditionally identified an [**empirical precedence** to
egotistical]{.underline} collective [units such as]{.underline}---and
most often--- [states and their **protection** of
sovereignty]{.underline} on the national scale [when relating to other
**similar** entities through]{.underline} amity or
**[enmity]{.underline}** (Buzan and Wæver 2009, 254--55). This does not,
however, indicate that referent objects at higher or lower scales than
the national are not relevant to securitization processes. Instead, it
is a consequence of the challenging process of successfully declaring,
for example, 'humankind' on the system level or 'human being' on the
individual level as threatened and particularly devising meaningful
extraordinary means to their protection (Buzan and Wæver 2009, 254--55).
This is because the relevant audience, the securitizing actor's scope of
power and, hence, the possible extraordinary means are here not as
clearly defined as they are within the boundaries of a state, which
remains the most powerful unit in Buzan and Wæver's analysis. In their
investigation into what happens above2 the scale of collective units,
[Buzan and Wæver show how international [security]{.mark}]{.underline}
in a few instances [[is structured by]{.mark} [**one** single]{.mark}
overarching [**conflict** that]{.mark}]{.underline} "[**incorporate**,
**[align]{.mark}** and **rank** [the]{.mark} more [**parochial
securitisations** beneath it]{.mark}]{.underline}" (Buzan and Wæver
2009, 253), hence obtaining the status [[of]{.underline}]{.mark} a
so-called [[macrosecuritization]{.underline}]{.mark}. The
[[example]{.underline}]{.mark} par excellence [[is]{.underline}]{.mark}
the [[Cold]{.mark} [War]{.mark}]{.underline},
[[where]{.underline}]{.mark}as [[the **Global War on
Terror**]{.underline}]{.mark} and the current climate change
**[discourse]{.underline}** are [located on a **high
scale**]{.underline} though not being as all-encompassing due to a
lesser degree of widespread acceptance (Buzan and Wæver 2009, 254; see
Kristensen and Mortensgaard, chap. 2, this vol.). In our analysis, these
three [[major securitizations **constitute**
inevitable]{.underline}]{.mark} elements of the historical context
coconstituting U.S. engagements in Greenland. We will show how
articulations of these overarching conflicts have differed throughout
history due to their perceived relevance but also depending on what the
relevant audience would accept as an existential threat.

Our second theoretical point of attention is **[[cascading
effects]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [which]{.underline} so far are
remarkably seldom highlighted in securitization analyses, though such
observations are straightforward to make (cf. Wæver 2017, 126). Thierry
Balzacq and Ulrik Pram Gad are among the few who have previously done
so, but their uses of the concept differ from one another. In Balzacq's
(2010, 37) inquiry into methods of securitization theory, he uses the
concept to explain the cascading effects [within a **recipient**
audience]{.underline}, [[where people]{.underline}]{.mark} may be
[[**convinced** by their friend's **acceptance** of a securitization
**act** and]{.underline}]{.mark} may subsequently [[**convince**
others]{.underline}]{.mark}, [hence [**continuing** the **cascade** of
acceptance]{.mark}]{.underline} and, one may add, possible refusal of a
securitization attempt. In another perspective, Ulrik Pram Gad (2021,
125--27) has used the concept to show how one
[securitization]{.underline} may [[trigger a
**second**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[which]{.underline}]{.mark} further
[[activates a **third**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[and **so**
on]{.underline}]{.mark}, in his analysis of translations of security
cascading at the Thule Air Base. With inspiration from Gad, we seek to
develop his approach by pointing attention to two kinds of cascading
securitizations, namely vertical and horizontal ones:3 The vertical
cascading effect of a securitization is the situation when a
securitization articulated at one scale moves to a lower scale, with
consequences beyond the initial intention of the securitizing actor.
[**[Horizontal]{.mark}** cascading [effects]{.mark}]{.underline} instead
[describe]{.underline} the movement from one sector to another as for
example [[when the **transformative** force of]{.mark}]{.underline}
climate [[change triggers **insecurity** in several
other]{.underline}]{.mark}4 [[sectors]{.underline}]{.mark} (cf. Jacobsen
and Herrmann 2017, 7), [[such as]{.mark} moving from the]{.underline}
environmental to the societal, [economic]{.underline}, or even military
[sector if **[resource scarcity]{.mark}** leads to
conflict]{.underline}. The horizontal and vertical
[[cascades]{.underline}]{.mark} may [[happen at the same
time]{.underline}]{.mark}, such as [[when]{.mark} an **[international
securitization]{.mark}**]{.underline} of climate change [[also affects
**national** policies]{.mark} that]{.underline} further [cascade onto
**local** households]{.underline}, perhaps by setting new requirements
for their daily living. As our analysis will show, there have been
examples when a U.S. securitization has been [articulated
as]{.underline} part of a **[[macrosecuritization]{.underline}]{.mark}**
to legitimize extraordinary means, which subsequently has had vertical
cascading effects on the Arctic region, Greenland, and its population,
while simultaneously cascading horizontally from one sector onto several
others.

### L\-\--SetCol\-\--1NC

#### Greenlandic mining revives settler colonial logics as Denmark infantilizes Inuit governance, portraying resource development as dangerous without white supervision.

**Sejersen '19** \[Frank; August 8; Department of Cross-Cultural and
Regional Studies, University of Copenhagen; Polar Record, "Brokers of
hope: Extractive industries and the dynamics of future-making in
post-colonial Greenland," 10.1017/S0032247419000457\] //jwh

The [[routes]{.underline}]{.mark} and potentialities [[created
by]{.mark} the construction of a]{.underline} "**[[future
us]{.underline}]{.mark}**" are [[**deeply** situated
in]{.underline}]{.mark} post-**[colonial
[discou]{.mark}rse]{.underline}**, and recurrently the
Danish-Greenlandic affective relations take centre stage in discussions
and are played out in new ways (Gad, 2009, 2016) -- also by brokers of
hope. The colonial relationship has often been compared to a parent and
child relationship, and this metaphor was used creatively by the brokers
of hope to establish ideas of new beginnings through new partnerships
(with the Chinese). Much of the historical relationship between Denmark
and Greenland can be characterised as a relationship where Denmark took
the role and saw itself as protector and introducer of modernity.
According to Paine (1977), the [[relationship between **Inuit** and
**white** people]{.mark}]{.underline} generally has been [[characterised
by]{.underline}]{.mark} a [[**superior** attitude by the whites]{.mark}
who]{.underline} have [pointed at their]{.underline} technical,
[**political**, **moral** and **cultural** superiority [to
**legitimise** colonial **interventions**]{.mark}]{.underline} and
attitudes. [This [image of oneself as superior]{.mark}]{.underline} and
a counter image of the Inuit have [[produced]{.mark} an [**othering**
process where Inuit have been related to]{.mark}]{.underline} on the
basis of [[the **child metaphor**]{.underline}]{.mark} (Gad, 2016;
Langgård, 2013; Rud, 2014; Thisted, 2012; Thomsen, 1998). This child
metaphor results in a number of related understandings of the Inuit:
[they need to **learn** from the **whites**, they need to
be]{.underline} tutored, [**guided** and helped]{.underline} and to be
protected from the world until mature enough to engage in it.
Consequently, [the gradual **historical** change from **colonial
dependence** to]{.underline} more [self-determination]{.underline} has
often been [phrased as]{.underline} similar to [a **maturing
child**]{.underline} getting ready to move away from home -- away from
the well-meaning care, embracing protection and supervision of parents
(Lindroth & Sinevaara-Niskanen, 2018). The relationship between Denmark
and Greenland can be understood by the concept of pastoral power, where
Inuit in their helplessness (as seen from Denmark) are taken under the
motherly wings of the Danes, brought into the modern world with the best
intentions and rewarded when following the new standards and values.
[[In **Arctic literature**, this image of **Inuit** as **childlike**
is]{.underline}]{.mark} extremely [[widespread]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Vallee (1962) was one of the first to describe this relation, and
Parsons (1970, p. 38) noted in his study of whites in the Canadian North
the "widespread conviction that natives tend to behave in a manner which
is essentially child-like, and thus like children, they must be taught
to exercise initiative, develop self-reliance, practice self-restraint,
and generally cope with a complex, changing world". [This **child-like**
position]{.underline} furthermore [[links to the **perception** of
Inuit]{.mark} as **closer** to nature]{.underline} compared to Western
people. Being "children of nature" and "children of the moment" (Müller,
1906) they are, of course, to [[face problems]{.mark} [when they
meet]{.mark}]{.underline} the giants of modernisation be it the big city
[or the transnational **[mining]{.mark}** company]{.underline}. This
presupposed childish attitude has been a permanent theme in writings
about the Arctic -- and it is still today. Ann Fienup-Riordan (1990,
1995) terms the production of this image Arctic Orientalism. [It is this
complex]{.underline} post-**[[colonial]{.underline}]{.mark}** political
and affective [field that the [**brokers** of hope]{.mark} need to
**navigate**]{.underline} diligently.

A [**Greenland** faced with]{.underline} an industrial [**future**
[driven b]{.mark}y]{.underline} large [transnational corporations with
[huge]{.mark}]{.underline} Chinese [[investments]{.underline} [and
**thousands** of workers]{.underline}]{.mark} has [[created]{.mark} a
**[fear]{.mark}**]{.underline} in Denmark that [the Greenlanders will
**not** be able to **handle** the situation]{.underline} -- at least not
on their own and without help. If not helped, [[Greenland would be
turned]{.mark} [into a]{.mark}]{.underline} Chinese **[[work
camp]{.underline}]{.mark}** as mentioned in a Danish newspaper
(Jyllands-Posten, 2013). Despite the fact that Greenland under the
Self-rule Act has the right, possibility and responsibility to manage
the its non-renewable resources, the Danes maintain to be concerned. In
2013 (May 20), the former Danish foreign minister Uffe Elleman Jensen
raised concern about the Arctic interests shown by China and other Asian
countries. He concluded that it was about time that Denmark and
Greenland joined forces to protect themselves against "[the many [**big
hungry wolfs** that **forage** the landscape]{.mark} on the
[**look-out** for]{.mark} opportunities to get **close** to the natural
[**wealth** that everyone dreams about
**exploiting**]{.mark}]{.underline}" (translated by the author,
Ellemann, 2013).

He had three focus points: (1) China has [**[predator
interests]{.mark}** in the Arctic]{.underline}; (2) the West should
protect itself, its sovereignty and its resources from this threat; (3)
Greenland and Denmark should cooperate to withstand the pressure from
the outside.

By stating this, he joined the choir of Danes that expressed concern
about Greenlandic wealth that may be grabbed by foreign firms --
especially Chinese. The problem was understood as [[**more** than just
**profit**]{.underline}]{.mark} that [would run out of the Danish
Kingdom]{.underline}. It was also phrased as a security problem that
China potentially would get access and control over resources important
for the Western world. In line with this way of thinking, the Danish
political party Dansk Folkeparti encouraged (and even demanded) that
Denmark and Western firms entered the Greenlandic scene in order to
secure the resources and Western interests from Chinese dominance. If
not done, Dansk Folkeparti claimed, the Chinese [[would have **huge
power** over]{.mark} a **large part** of [the world]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Ritzau, 2013b). Dansk Folkeparti wanted Denmark to take responsibility
immediately -- "[otherwise **history** will judge us hard]{.underline}",
as the party's spokesperson announced. It seemed as if Denmark really
needed a wakeup call, and the Danish admiral Nils Wang also added
that"the EU and the US have been utterly inattentive"(translated by the
author, as cited in Halskov & Davidsen-Nielsen, 2012) and thus given
China the best opportunities. Foreign experts aired the same concern
about Chinese expansion. In a warning statement from Hugh Stephens in
The Diplomat, he underlined that "one thing is certain: China's
[[**interests** in the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}, whether [regarding
possibilities [for **expanded**]{.mark}]{.underline} navigation and
shipping, [access to [resources]{.mark}]{.underline}, concerns over the
environmental impact of the melting ice packs or possibly even defense
and security issues in the region, [are **[only going to
grow]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Canada (and the US) would be wise to take
note" (Stephens, 2012).
